Objection That Existence Is a Type-Nature and Its Refutation
Sanusi raises and refutes the objection that existence is a type-nature, using the example of light and concluding that existence is predicated analogically.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why conceptual unity of existence does not imply it is a type-nature with uniform properties, and how Sanusi refutes this objection using the analogy of light.

Core Idea
Existence is not a type-nature; different realities can share a concept without uniform implications.

This lesson presents a key objection in metaphysics: if existence is a single concept common to all beings, then it must be a type-nature with uniform essential properties. Sanusi rejects this, using the analogy of light to show that a single concept can apply to different realities with different properties. We'll walk through the objection, the response, and the deeper point about predication.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection: Existence as a type-nature
The objector argues that because existence is one concept, its properties must be constant. This is a common rationalist argument.
Notice the phrase 'tabi'a naw'iyya' (type-nature).
2
Read Sanusi's initial denial and the analogy of light
He says 'mamnu' (denied) and gives the example of different lights, showing that a single concept can have varying implications.
Light (nur) applies to sunlight and other lights, but sunlight enables the night-blind to see, unlike others.
3
Understand the deeper proof attributed to the Imam
The objection is actually a more sophisticated argument that cannot be dismissed by simple denial. The text presents this as 'ishaara ila daleel aakhar'.
This proof argues that if existence is a single concept, it must be a type-nature with uniform implications.
4
Study the final response: Existence is predicated analogically
The answer argues that existence is not a type-nature but a non-essential attribute, and that it is predicated analogically (mushakkik) rather than univocally.
Existence applies to the Necessary more fittingly, strongly, and priorly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : الوجود طبيعة نوعية فلا تختلف لوازمها . قلت : ممنوع بل الوجودات متحالفة بالحقيقة يجب للبعض منها ما يمتنع على البعض كالأنوار ويقع المطلق عليها وقوع لازم غير ذاتي لها . نوعية لما إشارة إلى دليل آخر للإمام لا يندفع بما ذكر تقريره أن الوجود طبيعة بينتم من كونه مفهوماً واحداً مشتركاً بين الكل، والطبيعة النوعية لا تختلف لوازمها بل يجب لكل فرد منها ما يجب للآخر، لامتناع تخلف المقتضى عن المقتضى، وعلى هذا بنيتم كثيراً من القواعد كما سيأتي . فالوجود إن اقتضى العروض أو اللا عروض لم يختلف ذلك في الواجب والممكن، وإن لم يقتض شيئاً منهما ما احتاج الواجب في وجوبه إلى منفصل كما سبق . والجواب : أنما لا نسلم أنه طبيعة نوعية ومجرد اتحاد المفهوم لا يوجب ذلك لجواز أن يصدق مفهوم واحد على أشياء مختلفة الحقيقة واللوازم كالنور يصدق على نور الشمس وغيره مع أنه يقتضي إبصار الأعشى بخلاف سائر الأنوار، فيجوز أن تكون الوجودات الخاصة متخالفة بالحقيقة يجب للوجود الواجب التجرد ويمتنع عليه المقارنة، والممكن بالعكس مع اشتراك الكل في صدق مفهوم الوجود المطلق عليها صدق العرضي اللازم على معروضاته الملزومة، كالنور على الأنوار لا يصدق الذاتي بمعنى تمام الحقيقة، ليكون طبيعة نوعية كالإنسان لأفراده أو بمعنى جزء الماهية، ليلزم التركب كالحيوان لأنواعه . قال : متواطئاً أو مشككاً وهو الحق لكونه في الواجب أولى وأشد وأقدم .
If it is said: Existence (al-wujud) is a type-nature (tabi'a naw'iyya), so its essential properties (lawazim) do not differ. I say: It is denied; rather existences are different in reality, and what is necessary for some of them is impossible for others, like lights (al-anwar), and the absolute (al-mutlaq) applies to them as a non-essential necessary attribute (lazim ghayr dhati). As for being a nature, it is an indication of another proof of the Imam that is not refuted by what was mentioned. His exposition is that existence is a nature because it is a single concept common to all, and a type-nature does not have differing essential properties; rather what is necessary for one individual is necessary for another, because the consequent cannot lag behind the antecedent. On this you have built many rules as will come. So if existence entails being an accident or not being an accident, that would not differ between the necessary and the possible. And if it entails neither, then the necessary would need something separate for its necessity as before. The answer: We do not accept that it is a type-nature, and mere conceptual unity does not necessitate that, because it is possible for a single concept to apply to things differing in reality and essential properties, like light applies to sunlight and other lights, while sunlight necessitates causing the night-blind to see, unlike other lights. So it is possible that specific existences differ in reality, with the existence of the necessary requiring abstraction and being impossible of composition, while the possible is the opposite, despite all sharing the application of the concept of absolute existence as a necessary accident applies to its substrates, like light to lights, not as an essential (dhati) meaning complete reality, to be a type-nature like human for its individuals, nor as part of the quiddity, to entail composition like animal for its species. He said: Univocally (mutawati'an) or analogically (mushakkikan), and it is the truth because in the necessary it is more fitting, stronger, and prior.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage presents an objection: Existence (al-wujud) is a type-nature (tabi'a naw'iyya), meaning it is a single common concept that must have uniform essential properties (lawazim) for all its instances. Therefore, whatever applies to existence in one case applies in all. The answer denies this: existences are different in reality, and what is necessary for some (e.g., the Necessary Being) may be impossible for others (e.g., contingent beings), using the analogy of lights (anwar) where sunlight differs from other lights in its effect of enabling the night-blind to see. The commentator explains that this objection actually points to another proof by the Imam (likely al-Razi) that cannot be refuted by the simple denial. The proof argues that since existence is a single concept common to all instances, it must be a type-nature, and thus its implications are uniform. The response provides a detailed answer: we do not accept that conceptual unity makes it a type-nature. A single concept can apply to different realities, like the term 'light' applies to various luminous entities despite different properties. Therefore, specific existences can differ: the existence of the Necessary is abstract and cannot be composite, while the possible is the opposite, even though all share the concept of absolute existence as a non-essential attribute (aradiy) that is a necessary concomitant (lazim) of its subjects, not as an essential (dhati) or part of the quiddity. The passage ends with a point about whether existence is predicated univocally (mutawati'an) or analogically (mushakkikan), stating that the latter is correct because existence in the Necessary is more fitting, stronger, and prior.

Key Terms
طبيعة نوعية (tabi'a naw'iyya)
لوازم (lawazim)
الوجودات (al-wujudat)
الأنوار (al-anwar)
لازم غير ذاتي (lazim ghayr dhati)
متواطئ (mutawati')
مشكك (mushakkik)
العرضي اللازم (al-'aradi al-lazim)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because existence is a single concept, it must be a type-nature with uniform essential properties.
Correction: The response clarifies that conceptual unity does not entail type-nature; different realities can share a concept as a non-essential attribute, as with light and different lights.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the answer use to show that a single concept can apply to different realities?
A) Human and animal
B) Light and various lights
C) Necessary and possible
D) Substance and accident

Key Takeaway
Existence is not a type-nature; conceptual unity does not entail uniform essential properties, and existence is predicated analogically, applying more eminently to the Necessary Being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Sanusi shows that Allah's existence is not just another instance of existence like yours—it is prior, stronger, and more fitting. Do you truly internalize that your own existence is utterly dependent and borrowed, while His is self-sufficient and necessary? How does this affect the way you turn to Him in need?
?
The light analogy teaches that a single concept can apply to different realities with vastly different properties. Have you ever subconsciously imagined Allah's existence as 'the same kind of existence' as yours, only greater? How can you train yourself to think of His existence as incomparable and transcendent, as the lesson demands?
?
If existence is not a type-nature, then your existence and all created existences are radically contingent. Does this knowledge make you feel fragile and dependent? How will you respond today by consciously acknowledging your need for the Real Existence (al-Wajib al-Wujud)?

8 min
The Claim of Existence as a Generic Nature
Taftazani presents al-Razi's argument and his first refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's argument that existence is a generic nature and Taftazani's initial denial using the analogy of light.

Core Idea
A shared concept does not prove a shared nature.

Imam al-Razi argued that existence must be a generic nature because it is a single concept common to all existents. Taftazani begins his refutation by denying this premise, using the example of light to show that a single concept can apply to things with different realities and concomitants.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its translation. Note the structure: presentation of the Imam's argument, then the answer.
2
Identify the Imam's argument
Find the claim that existence is a generic nature and the reasoning behind it: unity of concept, uniformity of concomitants.
The argument is based on the impossibility of a requisite being absent from its requisite.
3
Understand the light analogy
Taftazani gives the example of 'light' applying to sunlight and other lights, yet they have different properties. This shows that conceptual unity does not imply generic nature.
Sunlight cures night-blindness; other lights do not.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Existence is a necessary accident (arad lazim) predicated of different realities, not a constitutive nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إشارة إلى دليل آخر للإمام لا يندفع بما ذكر تقريره أن الوجود طبيعة نوعية بينتم من كونه مفهوماً واحداً مشتركاً بين الكل، والطبيعة النوعية لا تختلف لوازمها بل يجب لكل فرد منها ما يجب للآخر، لامتناع تخلف المقتضى عن المقتضى، وعلى هذا بنيتم كثيراً من القواعد كما سيأتي . فالوجود إن اقتضى العروض أو اللا عروض لم يختلف ذلك في الواجب والممكن، وإن لم يقتض شيئاً منهما ما احتاج الواجب في وجوبه إلى منفصل كما سبق . والجواب : أنما لا نسلم أنه طبيعة نوعية ومجرد اتحاد المفهوم لا يوجب ذلك لجواز أن يصدق مفهوم واحد على أشياء مختلفة الحقيقة واللوازم كالنور يصدق على نور الشمس وغيره مع أنه يقتضي إبصار الأعشى بخلاف سائر الأنوار، فيجوز أن تكون الوجودات الخاصة متخالفة بالحقيقة يجب للوجود الواجب التجرد ويمتنع عليه المقارنة، والممكن بالعكس مع اشتراك الكل في صدق مفهوم الوجود المطلق عليها صدق العرضي اللازم على معروضاته الملزومة، كالنور على الأنوار لا يصدق الذاتي بمعنى تمام الحقيقة، ليكون طبيعة نوعية كالإنسان لأفراده أو بمعنى جزء الماهية، ليلزم التركب كالحيوان لأنواعه . قال : متواطئاً أو مشككاً وهو الحق لكونه في الواجب أولى وأشد وأقدم . إشارة إلى أن الجواب يتم بما ذكرنا من المنع مستنداً بأنه يجوز اشتراك الملزومات المختلفة الحقائق في لازم واحد غير ذاتي، سواء كانت مقوليته عليها بالتواطؤ كالماهية على الماهيات والتشخص على التشخيصات، أو بالتشكيك كالبياض على البياضات والحرارة على الحرارات فلا يلزم من كون الوجود مفهوماً واحداً مشتركاً بين الوجودات كونه طبيعة نوعية والوجودات أفراداً متفقة الحقيقة واللوازم وإن فرضنا اشتراك الكل في مفهوم الوجود على السواء، من غير أولية ولا أولوية، إلا أنه لما كان الواقع هو التشكيك وكان من دأب الحكيم المحقق سلوك طريق التحقيق ذكر في جواب استدلالات الإمام أن الوجود مقول على الوجودات بالتشكيك، لأنه في العلة أقدم منه في المعلول وفي الجواهر أولى منه في العرض، وفي العرض القار كالسواد أشد منه في غير القار كالحركة، بل هو في الواجب أقدم وأولى وأشد منه في الممكن، والواقع على الأشياء بالتشكيك يكون عارضاً لها خارجاً عنها، لا ماهية لها أو جزء ماهية لامتناع اختلافهما على ما سيأتي، فلا يكون الوجود طبيعة نوعية للوجودات، بل لازماً خارجاً يقع على ما تحته بمعنى واحد، ولا يلزم من تساوي ملزوماته التي هي وجود الواجب ووجودات الممكنات في الحقيقة ليمتنع اختلافها في العروض واللاعروض، وفي المبدئية للممكنات وعدم المبدئية إلى غير ذلك. والعجب أن الإمام قد اطلع من كلام الفارابي وابن سينا على أن مرادهم أن حقيقة الواجب وجود مجرد، هي محض الواجبية لاشتراك فيه أصلا، والوجود المشترك العام المعلوم لازم له غير مقوم بل صرح في بعض كتبه بأن الوجود مقول على الوجودات بالتشكيك ثم استمر على شبهته التي زعم أنها من المتانة بحيث لا يمكن توجيه شك نحيل عليها
Reference to another argument of the Imam that is not repelled by what was mentioned: its presentation is that existence is a generic nature, because it is a single concept common to all, and a generic nature does not have differing concomitants; rather, for every individual of it what is necessary for the other is necessary, due to impossibility of the requisite being absent from the requisite. And upon this you have built many rules, as will come. So existence, if it necessitates being an accident or not being an accident, that would not differ in the Necessary and the possible; and if it does not necessitate any of them, then the Necessary would need in its necessity something separate, as before. The answer: we do not grant that it is a generic nature, and mere unity of concept does not necessitate that, because it is possible that a single concept be true of things differing in reality and concomitants, like light being true of sunlight and other light, while it necessitates sight for the night-blind unlike other lights. So it is possible that particular existences differ in reality: for the Necessary existence, non-accidentality is necessary and concomitance is impossible, and for the possible the opposite, while all share in the truth of the concept of absolute existence upon them, as a necessary accident upon its substrates which are its subject-matters, like light upon lights; it is not true as an intrinsic meaning of the reality, so as to be a generic nature like man for his individuals, or as part of the quiddity, so as to necessitate composition like animal for its species. He said: univocally or equivocally, and the truth is the latter, because it is in the Necessary prior and stronger and more ancient. An indication that the answer is completed by what we mentioned of denial, based on the permissibility of sharing of subject-matters differing in realities in a single non-essential concomitant, whether its predication upon them is by univocity, like quiddity upon quiddities and individuation upon individuations, or by equivocity, like whiteness upon whitenesses and heat upon heats. So it does not follow from existence being a single concept shared among existences that it is a generic nature and existences are individuals agreeing in reality and concomitants, even if we suppose all share equally in the concept of existence, without priority or preference. However, since reality is equivocity and it is the custom of the wise investigator to follow the path of verification, he mentioned in answer to the Imam's arguments that existence is predicated of existences by equivocity, because it is in the cause more prior than in the effect, and in substances more appropriate than in accident, and in the stable accident like blackness more intense than in the non-stable like movement; rather, it is in the Necessary more prior and more appropriate and more intense than in the possible. And what is predicated of things by equivocity is accidental to them and external to them, not their quiddity or part of the quiddity, because of the impossibility of their differing, as will come. So existence is not a generic nature for existences, but an external concomitant that falls upon what is under it in one meaning; and from the equality of its subject-matters, which are the existence of the Necessary and the existences of possibles, in reality, it does not follow that they cannot differ in being accidental or non-accidental, and in being originalive for possibles or not originalive, and so on. And it is surprising that the Imam, having seen from the words of al-Farabi and Ibn Sina that their intention is that the reality of the Necessary is sheer existence, pure necessity, with no sharing at all, and the common general existence known is a concomitant for Him, not constitutive—rather, he stated in some of his books that existence is predicated of existences by equivocity—then he persisted in his doubt which he claimed was of such solidity that no thin doubt can be directed at it.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an argument by Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi that existence (wujud) is a generic nature (tabi'a naw'iyya). The author, al-Taftazani, first presents the Imam's argument: existence is a single concept common to all existents; as a generic nature, its concomitants (lawazim) must be uniform across all individuals; otherwise the requisite (muqtada) would be absent from the requisite (muqtada). The Imam then infers that whether existence necessitates being an accident (arad) or not, the rule would be the same for the Necessary Existent and possibles; if it necessitates neither, then the Necessary would need an external cause. Taftazani answers by denying the premise: mere conceptual unity does not imply that existence is a generic nature. A single concept can be true of things with different realities and concomitants, like 'light' applying to sunlight and other lights, yet sunlight has the property of curing night-blindness while other lights do not. Likewise, particular existences (wujudat khassa) may differ in reality: the Necessary Existent must be non-accidental and cannot be concomitant, while possibles are the opposite. The shared concept of absolute existence is a necessary accident (arad lazim) predicated of its substrates (ma'rudat), not an intrinsic (dhati) property. It is not a genus like 'man' for individuals nor a differentia like 'animal' for species. Taftazani then quotes al-Razi (who originally said existence is said univocally or equivocally, with equivocity being true) to support his answer. He explains that the denial is based on the possibility that different realities share a single non-essential concomitant. This shared predicate can be either univocal (mutawati'), like 'quiddity' applied to various quiddities, or equivocal (mushakkik), like 'whiteness' applied to different shades. Therefore, from existence being a single concept it does not follow that it is a generic nature. In fact, the reality is tashkik (gradation): existence in the cause is more prior than in the effect, in substances more appropriate than in accidents, in stable accidents like blackness more intense than in non-stable like motion, and in the Necessary Existent even more prior, appropriate, and intense than in possibles. Predication by tashkik indicates that existence is accidental to the essences, not constitutive. Hence existence is not a generic nature but an external concomitant that applies in one meaning. Equality of its substrates (the Necessary and possibles) in the reality of existence does not entail they cannot differ in being accidental or non-accidental, or in being originalive or not. Taftazani ends with surprise that al-Razi, despite knowing from al-Farabi and Ibn Sina that the Necessary's reality is pure existence with no sharing, and that the common existence is a non-constitutive concomitant, and even stating in some books that existence is said by tashkik, still persisted in his weak doubt.

Key Terms
الوجود
طبيعة نوعية
العرض اللازم
الملزوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the univocity of the concept of existence implies existence is a genus shared equally by God and creatures.
Correction: The passage clarifies that a common concept can be an accident not a nature; existence is a necessary accident, not a constitutive nature.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the light analogy in Taftazani's refutation?
A) Light is a generic nature for all lights.
B) A single concept can be true of things with different properties, so existence need not be a generic nature.
C) Sunlight and other lights share the same reality.
D) Existence is like light in being a substance.

Key Takeaway
Mere unity of concept does not entail generic nature; the same concept can be an accidental predicate of diverse realities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of your own existence, do you subconsciously treat it as something you possess independently, like a nature inherent to you? How does Taftazani's argument that existence is only an accident challenge that feeling of ownership?
?
If the concept 'existence' applies to God and to you in a purely accidental way, what does that mean for your daily reliance on your own being? Are you more attached to the accident than to the One who truly is existence itself?
?
The light analogy shows that a single name can hide vast differences in reality. In what areas of your faith do you assume that shared terms (e.g., 'mercy', 'power') imply shared natures between God and creation? How must you recalibrate your understanding after this lesson?

Univocity, Equivocity, and the Proof of Tashkik
Taftazani discusses univocal vs. equivocal predication and argues for tashkik.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between univocal and equivocal predication and how Taftazani uses tashkik to establish that existence is not a generic nature.

Core Idea
Existence is predicated by gradation (tashkik), not univocally.

Building on the denial that existence is a generic nature, Taftazani introduces the concepts of univocal (mutawati') and equivocal (mushakkik) predication. He argues that even if existence were predicated univocally, it would still not imply a generic nature. However, the reality is that existence is predicated by tashkik—a graded, analogical sense—which further shows it is accidental and not constitutive.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on predication
Read the Arabic from 'قال : متواطئاً أو مشككاً' to 'لا يلزم من كون الوجود مفهوماً واحداً...' and its translation.
2
Distinguish univocal and equivocal
Understand the two types of predication: univocal (equal application) and equivocal (graded application). Examples are given.
Univocal: quiddity applied to quiddities; equivocal: whiteness applied to shades.
3
See why tashkik is the truth
Taftazani states that in reality, existence is predicated by tashkik because it is more prior, appropriate, and intense in the Necessary than in the possible.
This graded predication shows existence is accidental, not essential.
4
Understand the conclusion
Therefore, existence is not a generic nature but an external concomitant that applies in one meaning but with degrees.

Source Text

. والجواب : أنما لا نسلم أنه طبيعة نوعية ومجرد اتحاد المفهوم لا يوجب ذلك لجواز أن يصدق مفهوم واحد على أشياء مختلفة الحقيقة واللوازم كالنور يصدق على نور الشمس وغيره مع أنه يقتضي إبصار الأعشى بخلاف سائر الأنوار، فيجوز أن تكون الوجودات الخاصة متخالفة بالحقيقة يجب للوجود الواجب التجرد ويمتنع عليه المقارنة، والممكن بالعكس مع اشتراك الكل في صدق مفهوم الوجود المطلق عليها صدق العرضي اللازم على معروضاته الملزومة، كالنور على الأنوار لا يصدق الذاتي بمعنى تمام الحقيقة، ليكون طبيعة نوعية كالإنسان لأفراده أو بمعنى جزء الماهية، ليلزم التركب كالحيوان لأنواعه . قال : متواطئاً أو مشككاً وهو الحق لكونه في الواجب أولى وأشد وأقدم . إشارة إلى أن الجواب يتم بما ذكرنا من المنع مستنداً بأنه يجوز اشتراك الملزومات المختلفة الحقائق في لازم واحد غير ذاتي، سواء كانت مقوليته عليها بالتواطؤ كالماهية على الماهيات والتشخص على التشخيصات، أو بالتشكيك كالبياض على البياضات والحرارة على الحرارات فلا يلزم من كون الوجود مفهوماً واحداً مشتركاً بين الوجودات كونه طبيعة نوعية والوجودات أفراداً متفقة الحقيقة واللوازم وإن فرضنا اشتراك الكل في مفهوم الوجود على السواء، من غير أولية ولا أولوية، إلا أنه لما كان الواقع هو التشكيك وكان من دأب الحكيم المحقق سلوك طريق التحقيق ذكر في جواب استدلالات الإمام أن الوجود مقول على الوجودات بالتشكيك، لأنه في العلة أقدم منه في المعلول وفي الجواهر أولى منه في العرض، وفي العرض القار كالسواد أشد منه في غير القار كالحركة، بل هو في الواجب أقدم وأولى وأشد منه في الممكن، والواقع على الأشياء بالتشكيك يكون عارضاً لها خارجاً عنها، لا ماهية لها أو جزء ماهية لامتناع اختلافهما على ما سيأتي، فلا يكون الوجود طبيعة نوعية للوجودات، بل لازماً خارجاً يقع على ما تحته بمعنى واحد، ولا يلزم من تساوي ملزوماته التي هي وجود الواجب ووجودات الممكنات في الحقيقة ليمتنع اختلافها في العروض واللاعروض، وفي المبدئية للممكنات وعدم المبدئية إلى غير ذلك. والعجب أن الإمام قد اطلع من كلام الفارابي وابن سينا على أن مرادهم أن حقيقة الواجب وجود مجرد، هي محض الواجبية لاشتراك فيه أصلا، والوجود المشترك العام المعلوم لازم له غير مقوم بل صرح في بعض كتبه بأن الوجود مقول على الوجودات بالتشكيك ثم استمر على شبهته التي زعم أنها من المتانة بحيث لا يمكن توجيه شك نحيل عليها
Reference to another argument of the Imam that is not repelled by what was mentioned: its presentation is that existence is a generic nature, because it is a single concept common to all, and a generic nature does not have differing concomitants; rather, for every individual of it what is necessary for the other is necessary, due to impossibility of the requisite being absent from the requisite. And upon this you have built many rules, as will come. So existence, if it necessitates being an accident or not being an accident, that would not differ in the Necessary and the possible; and if it does not necessitate any of them, then the Necessary would need in its necessity something separate, as before. The answer: we do not grant that it is a generic nature, and mere unity of concept does not necessitate that, because it is possible that a single concept be true of things differing in reality and concomitants, like light being true of sunlight and other light, while it necessitates sight for the night-blind unlike other lights. So it is possible that particular existences differ in reality: for the Necessary existence, non-accidentality is necessary and concomitance is impossible, and for the possible the opposite, while all share in the truth of the concept of absolute existence upon them, as a necessary accident upon its substrates which are its subject-matters, like light upon lights; it is not true as an intrinsic meaning of the reality, so as to be a generic nature like man for his individuals, or as part of the quiddity, so as to necessitate composition like animal for its species. He said: univocally or equivocally, and the truth is the latter, because it is in the Necessary prior and stronger and more ancient. An indication that the answer is completed by what we mentioned of denial, based on the permissibility of sharing of subject-matters differing in realities in a single non-essential concomitant, whether its predication upon them is by univocity, like quiddity upon quiddities and individuation upon individuations, or by equivocity, like whiteness upon whitenesses and heat upon heats. So it does not follow from existence being a single concept shared among existences that it is a generic nature and existences are individuals agreeing in reality and concomitants, even if we suppose all share equally in the concept of existence, without priority or preference. However, since reality is equivocity and it is the custom of the wise investigator to follow the path of verification, he mentioned in answer to the Imam's arguments that existence is predicated of existences by equivocity, because it is in the cause more prior than in the effect, and in substances more appropriate than in accident, and in the stable accident like blackness more intense than in the non-stable like movement; rather, it is in the Necessary more prior and more appropriate and more intense than in the possible. And what is predicated of things by equivocity is accidental to them and external to them, not their quiddity or part of the quiddity, because of the impossibility of their differing, as will come. So existence is not a generic nature for existences, but an external concomitant that falls upon what is under it in one meaning; and from the equality of its subject-matters, which are the existence of the Necessary and the existences of possibles, in reality, it does not follow that they cannot differ in being accidental or non-accidental, and in being originalive for possibles or not originalive, and so on. And it is surprising that the Imam, having seen from the words of al-Farabi and Ibn Sina that their intention is that the reality of the Necessary is sheer existence, pure necessity, with no sharing at all, and the common general existence known is a concomitant for Him, not constitutive—rather, he stated in some of his books that existence is predicated of existences by equivocity—then he persisted in his doubt which he claimed was of such solidity that no thin doubt can be directed at it.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an argument by Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi that existence (wujud) is a generic nature (tabi'a naw'iyya). The author, al-Taftazani, first presents the Imam's argument: existence is a single concept common to all existents; as a generic nature, its concomitants (lawazim) must be uniform across all individuals; otherwise the requisite (muqtada) would be absent from the requisite (muqtada). The Imam then infers that whether existence necessitates being an accident (arad) or not, the rule would be the same for the Necessary Existent and possibles; if it necessitates neither, then the Necessary would need an external cause. Taftazani answers by denying the premise: mere conceptual unity does not imply that existence is a generic nature. A single concept can be true of things with different realities and concomitants, like 'light' applying to sunlight and other lights, yet sunlight has the property of curing night-blindness while other lights do not. Likewise, particular existences (wujudat khassa) may differ in reality: the Necessary Existent must be non-accidental and cannot be concomitant, while possibles are the opposite. The shared concept of absolute existence is a necessary accident (arad lazim) predicated of its substrates (ma'rudat), not an intrinsic (dhati) property. It is not a genus like 'man' for individuals nor a differentia like 'animal' for species. Taftazani then quotes al-Razi (who originally said existence is said univocally or equivocally, with equivocity being true) to support his answer. He explains that the denial is based on the possibility that different realities share a single non-essential concomitant. This shared predicate can be either univocal (mutawati'), like 'quiddity' applied to various quiddities, or equivocal (mushakkik), like 'whiteness' applied to different shades. Therefore, from existence being a single concept it does not follow that it is a generic nature. In fact, the reality is tashkik (gradation): existence in the cause is more prior than in the effect, in substances more appropriate than in accidents, in stable accidents like blackness more intense than in non-stable like motion, and in the Necessary Existent even more prior, appropriate, and intense than in possibles. Predication by tashkik indicates that existence is accidental to the essences, not constitutive. Hence existence is not a generic nature but an external concomitant that applies in one meaning. Equality of its substrates (the Necessary and possibles) in the reality of existence does not entail they cannot differ in being accidental or non-accidental, or in being originalive or not. Taftazani ends with surprise that al-Razi, despite knowing from al-Farabi and Ibn Sina that the Necessary's reality is pure existence with no sharing, and that the common existence is a non-constitutive concomitant, and even stating in some books that existence is said by tashkik, still persisted in his weak doubt.

Key Terms
التشكيك
التواطؤ
العرض اللازم
الملزوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That univocal predication of existence would imply it is a genus.
Correction: Taftazani shows that even univocal predication of a non-essential concomitant does not imply generic nature; still less does equivocal predication.

Checkpoint
According to Taftazani, why does predication by tashkik show that existence is not a generic nature?
A) Because tashkik implies existence is a substance.
B) Because tashkik shows existence is accidental and external to the essence.
C) Because tashkik means existence is univocal.
D) Because tashkik requires that existence be a part of quiddity.

Key Takeaway
Existence is predicated by gradation (tashkik), proving it is an accidental concomitant, not a generic nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You affirm that Allah's existence is more prior, more appropriate, and more intense than your own existence. Yet, do you live as if His existence is the primary reality? When you fear the opinions of people more than His displeasure, or when you prioritize worldly comfort over His command, you treat their existence as more real. What is one specific area where you can shift your focus to reflect the gradation of existence taught in this lesson, starting today?
?
If existence is an accidental concomitant and not your essence, then your identity is not defined by existence but by what you are — a servant of Allah. Does your self-worth rely more on your existence (e.g., being known, having status) or on your servitude to Him? What would it look like to derive your worth solely from fulfilling His purpose for you? Name one concrete action you will take this week to align your self-concept with this truth.
?
The passage argues that existence in the Necessary is aqdam (more prior) than in the possible. In your daily thoughts, do you consider the reality of the Hereafter as more 'real' than this fleeting world? When you sin, do you act as if this world is ultimately more certain than the accountability to come? What step can you take today to make the priority of the Hereafter visible in your choices?

Critique of al-Razi's Inconsistency
Taftazani criticizes al-Razi's inconsistency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Taftazani's critique that al-Razi, despite knowing the correct view from al-Farabi and Ibn Sina, persisted in his flawed argument.

Core Idea
Al-Razi contradicted his own sources and his earlier statements.

Taftazani concludes with a surprising note: Imam al-Razi, having read al-Farabi and Ibn Sina, understood that the Necessary Existent is pure existence with no sharing, and even stated in some books that existence is predicated by tashkik, yet he still clung to his weak argument. This highlights the importance of consistency in theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the concluding passage
Read the final paragraph from 'والعجب أن الإمام' to the end, along with its translation.
2
Identify al-Razi's knowledge
Al-Razi knew from al-Farabi and Ibn Sina that the Necessary's reality is pure existence, and common existence is a non-constitutive concomitant.
3
Note the contradiction
Al-Razi even stated in some books that existence is predicated by tashkik, yet he persisted in his doubt.
Taftazani finds this surprising.
4
Understand the lesson
The story serves as a warning against stubbornness in argument and the importance of following verification.

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an argument by Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi that existence (wujud) is a generic nature (tabi'a naw'iyya). What does Taftazani find surprising about Imam al-Razi?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held onto a doubt or a belief despite knowing a truth that contradicts it? What kept you from changing your mind, and what would it take for you to let go?
?
Are there areas in your life—your faith, character, or daily habits—where you persist in something even though you know better? What would real consistency cost you, and are you willing to pay that price?
?
This lesson shows a great scholar contradicting himself. Do you examine your own positions for consistency, or do you avoid the discomfort of rethinking? When was the last time you surrendered a comfortable error for an uncomfortable truth?

Objection and Response on the Distinctness of Existences
Objection and answer about distinctness of existences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two meanings of 'distinctness' (tabāyun) and why sharing a universal concept does not imply identity of individuals.

Core Idea
Sharing a concept does not make things identical.

In this passage, the author addresses an objection about how distinct existences can share in existence. The objection assumes that if existences are distinct, they cannot share anything. The author clarifies that 'distinctness' can mean different things. Understanding this distinction is crucial for preventing confusion about universals and particulars.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: objection ('If it is said...') and response ('We say...').
The passage is a classic objection-and-answer unit.
2
Identify the two meanings of 'distinctness'
The author distinguishes two senses: non-identity (adam al-tasāduq) and no participation in the concept of existence. Understand how each sense is treated.
3
Analyze the example of 'al-māshī' (walking)
The example shows that individuals of different types share a universal concept without being identical. Reflect on how this parallels existences.
This example is key to understanding the argument.
4
Understand why sharing a concept does not entail identity
The author argues that sharing in the concept of existence does not require that existences be identical (tasāduq). This is the core refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: فتتباين الوجودات؟ قلنا: بمعنى عدم التصادق غير محال، وبمعنى عدم المشاركة في مفهوم الكون غير لازم، كأفراد الماشي. فإن أريد بالتباين عدم صدق بعضها على البعض فلا تسلم استحالته، وما ثبت من اشتراك الكل في مفهوم الوجود لا يقتضي تصادقها، وإن أريد عدم المشاركة في شيء أصلاً فلا تسلم لزومه، وما ذكر من عدم الاشتراك إلا في تمام الحقيقة أو بعض الذاتيات لا ينفي الاشتراك في عارض هو مفهوم الكون، كأفراد الماشي مع اختلاف أنواعها وأشخاصها: تشترك في المفهوم الكلي من غير تصادق بينها.
If it is said: Then existences are distinct? We say: If by distinct is meant non-identity (adam al-tasāduq), it is not impossible; and if by distinct is meant no participation in the concept of existence (mafhūm al-kawn), it is not necessary, like individuals of 'walking' (al-māshī). For if by distinctness is meant that some do not apply to others, its impossibility is not conceded; and what is established of the sharing of all in the concept of existence does not entail their mutual application. And if by distinctness is meant no participation in anything at all, its necessity is not conceded; and what was mentioned of no sharing except in the complete essence or some essential parts does not negate sharing in an accident which is the concept of existence, like the individuals of walking despite the difference of their types and persons: they share in the universal concept without mutual application.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The objector argues that if existences are distinct (tabāyun), then they cannot share in existence. The author responds by distinguishing two senses of 'distinctness': (1) non-identity of individuals (in which case sharing in a concept is not precluded) and (2) no commonality at all (which is not entailed). The example of 'al-māshī' (walking) illustrates that individuals of very different types share a common concept without being identical. Thus, the objection fails.

Key Terms
التباين (tabāyun)
التصادق (tasāduq)
المفهوم الكلي (mafhūm kullī)
الوجود (wujūd)
الكون (kawn)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two things share a concept (like existence), they must be identical or have the same essence.
Correction: Sharing a universal concept does not entail identity; individuals can be distinct while participating in the same concept.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main point about sharing in the concept of existence (wujud)?
A) All existences are the same in reality.
B) Sharing in the concept does not entail that the individuals are identical.
C) Distinctness is impossible because all things share in existence.
D) The concept of existence is a genus for all existents.

Key Takeaway
Distinct existences can share the universal concept of existence without being identical, just as different types of walkers share the concept of walking.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at other Muslims who differ from you in practice or opinion, do you assume they share nothing with you? This lesson teaches that sharing a concept (like Islam) does not mean identity. How can you apply this to your judgment of others, starting today?
?
You often claim to believe in one God, but do you treat the concept of 'God' as if it were a personal possession that makes you identical to others? What would change if you truly understood that sharing the concept does not erase individual responsibility and distinct relationship with Him?
?
Reflect on a time you felt threatened by someone's disagreement because you thought they had nothing in common with you. How might this lesson's distinction between 'non-identity' and 'no commonality' transform your approach to that person this week?

The Four Arguments Against Existence Being Additional to Essence
Enumeration of four arguments against addition of existence to essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four arguments that the identity theorists use to show that existence cannot be additional to essence, and why each argument leads to a contradiction or infinite regress.

Core Idea
Four logical arguments demonstrate that adding existence to essence leads to contradiction or infinite regress.

The debate over whether existence is identical to essence or additional is foundational in kalam. The identity theorists (those who say existence is the same as essence) argue that addition leads to impossible consequences. Taftazani here lays out their four specific arguments, which become the basis for the later reduction to two.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the four arguments
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. As you read, note the four distinct arguments labeled 'الأول', 'الثاني', 'الثالث', 'الرابع'. Each argues that adding existence to essence leads to an impossibility.
The second argument includes an objection and response; this is part of the argument's defense.
2
Understand the logical structure of each argument
Each argument examines what happens when existence subsists in essence (the subject). They consider whether the subject (essence) or the attribute (existence) is existent or non-existent, and derive a contradiction or regress.
The arguments are symmetric: first two focus on the subject, last two on the attribute.
3
Notice the common patterns: contradiction and regress
The four arguments boil down to two types of impossibility: contradiction (e.g., existence subsisting in non-existence) and circularity/infinite regress (e.g., needing an infinite chain of existences).
This sets up the reduction to two arguments in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج القائلون بكون الوجود نفس الماهية في الواجب والممكنات جميعاً بأنه لو لم يكن وليس جزءاً منها بالاتفاق لكان زائداً عليها قائماً بها، قيام الصفة بالموصوف، وقيام الشيء بالشيء فرع ثبوتهما في أنفسهما، لأن ما لا كون له في نفسه لا يكون محلاً ولا في محل، وهذا بالنظر إلى الوجود والماهية ممتنع لأنه في جانب الماهية فإنها بذاتها تتحقق محلاً للوجود لفتقها إما بذلك الوجود فيلزم تقدم الشيء على نفسه، ضرورة تقدم وجود المعروض على العارض، وإما بوجود آخر فيلزم تسلسل الموجودات ضرورة، إلا أن هذا الوجود أيضاً عارض يقتضي السابقية. وأما في جانب الوجود فلأنه لو تحقق والتقدير أن تحقق الشيء بالوجود زائد عليه تسلسلت الوجودات فباعتبار الوجود والعدم في كل من المعروض والعارض أمكن الاحتجاج على امتناع زيادة الوجود على الماهية بأربعة أوجه: الأول: أنه لو قام بها وهي بدون الوجود معدومة لزم قيام الوجود بالمعدوم، وفيه جمع بين صفتي الوجود والعدم، وهو تناقض. الثاني: أنه لو قام بها لزم سبقها بالوجود كما في سائر المعروضات، فإن كان لذلك الموجودية هو الوجود الأول لزم الدور، وهو توقف قيام الوجود بالماهية على الماهية الموجودة المتوقفة على قيام ذلك الوجود بها، وإن كان غيره لزم التسلسل، إلا أن هذا الوجود أيضاً معارض يقتضي سبق الماهية عليه بوجود آخر وهلم جرا. قيل: هذا التسلسل مع امتناعه لما سيأتي من الأدلة، ولاستلزامه انحصار ما لا يتناهى بين حاصرين، الوجود والماهية يستلزم المدعي، وهو كون الوجود نفس الماهية، لأن قيام جميع الموجودات العارضة بالماهية يستلزم وجوداً لها غير عارض، وإلا لم يكن الجميع جميعاً. وفيه نظر، لأنا لا نسلم على تقدير التسلسل تحقق جميع لا يكون وراءه وجود آخر، بل كل جميع فرضت معروضها بواسطة وجود آخر عارض، لأن معنى هذا التسلسل عدم انتهاء الوجودات إلى وجود لا يكون وراءه وجود بينه وبين الماهية وجود آخر. الثالث: أن وجود الشيء لو كان زائداً عليه لما كان الوجود موجوداً ضرورة امتناع تسلسل الوجودات بل معدوماً، وفيه اتصاف الشيء بنقيضه وكون ما لا ثبوت له في نفسه ثابتاً في محله. الرابع: أنه لو قام بالماهية لكان موجوداً ضرورة امتناع اتصاف الشيء بنقيضه، وامتناع أن يثبت في المحل ما لا ثبوت له في نفسه، فننقل الكلام إلى وجوده ويتسلسل، لأن التقدير أن وجود كل شيء زائد عليه. والتحقيق يقتضي رد الوجوه الأربعة إلى وجهين بطريق الترديد بين الوجود والعدم، في جانبي المعروض والعارض، على ما أوردنا في المتن. الأول: أنه قام بالماهية. فالماهية المعروضة إما معدومة فيتناقض أو موجودة فيدور أو يتسلسل. وتقرير الثاني: أن الوجود العارض إما معدوم فيتصف الشيء بنقيضه ويثبت في المحل ما لا ثبوت له في نفسه وإما موجود فيزيد وجوده عليه وتتسلسل الوجودات.
Those who claim that existence is identical to essence in both the necessary and contingent beings argued that if it were not identical and not a part of it by agreement, it would be additional to it and subsisting in it, like the subsistence of an attribute in its subject; but the subsistence of a thing in another thing is dependent on both being established in themselves, because what has no self-subsistence cannot be a locus nor be in a locus. This is impossible with respect to existence and essence: because on the side of essence, it is by itself a locus for existence due to its need, either through that existence – thus entailing the priority of a thing over itself, since the existence of the subject must precede the attribute – or through another existence, thus entailing an infinite regress of existences necessarily, except that this existence also is an attribute that requires precedence. As for the side of existence, if it were realized while the assumption is that the realization of a thing is through existence additional to it, existences would regress infinitely. So by considering existence and non-existence in both the subject and the attribute, it is possible to argue for the impossibility of the addition of existence to essence through four aspects: First: that if it subsists in essence while essence without existence is non-existent, then existence would subsist in the non-existent, and this combines the two attributes of existence and non-existence, which is a contradiction. Second: that if it subsists in essence, essence would have to precede it in existence as in other subjects, for if the existence that belongs to it is the first existence, then there is circularity – because the subsistence of existence in essence depends on the existing essence, which itself depends on the subsistence of that existence in it. And if it is another existence, then an infinite regress follows, except that this existence also requires the precedence of essence over it through another existence, and so on. It was said: This regress, despite its impossibility for reasons to come and because it necessitates confining the infinite between two limits (existence and essence), implies what the objector claims – that existence is identical to essence – because the subsistence of all accidental existences in essence requires an existence for it that is not accidental, otherwise they would not all be together. But there is a problem: we do not accept that on the supposition of the regress there exists a totality beyond which there is no other existence; rather every assumed totality has its subject through another accidental existence, because the meaning of this regress is that existences do not end at an existence beyond which there is no existence between it and essence. Third: that if existence of a thing were additional to it, then existence would not be existent, due to the impossibility of the regress of existences, but rather non-existent, and this entails describing a thing by its opposite and having what has no self-subsistence subsist in its locus. Fourth: that if it subsists in essence, it would be existent due to the impossibility of describing a thing by its opposite and the impossibility of that which has no self-subsistence subsisting in a locus; then we shift the discussion to its existence and an infinite regress follows, because the assumption is that the existence of everything is additional to it. And the true investigation requires reducing the four aspects to two aspects by way of disjunction between existence and non-existence in both the subject and the attribute, as we mentioned in the text. First: that it subsists in essence, so the essence that is the subject is either non-existent – thus a contradiction – or existent – thus circularity or regress. Second: that the accidental existence is either non-existent – thus the thing is described by its opposite and that which has no self-subsistence subsists in a locus – or existent – thus its existence adds to it and existences regress infinitely.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage presents and analyzes arguments of those who claim that existence is identical to essence (i.e., that existence is not additional to essence) in both God and contingent beings. The author begins by explaining the proponents' core reasoning: if existence were additional to essence but not a part of it, it would have to subsist in essence like an attribute in its subject. However, this requires both the essence and the existence to be self-subsistent before the relation, which leads to a problem: either the essence serves as a locus for existence, but then existence would depend on an existing essence, causing circularity or infinite regress. Conversely, if existence itself is considered, then since existence is what makes things real, an additional existence would entail an infinite chain. The author then distills this into four explicit arguments against the addition of existence. The first argument is that if existence subsists in essence while essence is non-existent (without existence), then existence would subsist in nothing, combining existence and non-existence in one thing—a contradiction. The second argument is that for existence to subsist in essence, essence must already exist as a subject, leading either to circularity (if the existence that makes essence exist is the same as the one subsisting) or to an infinite regress (if a different existence is needed). An objection is raised against the regress argument: the regress itself is impossible, and its impossibility actually supports the opponent's claim that existence and essence are identical. The author notes a problem in this objection, pointing out that in a regress one never reaches a final totality beyond which there is no further existence. The third argument is that if existence were additional, then existence itself would not be existent (since that would need another existence, ad infinitum), so it would be non-existent—again a contradiction. The fourth argument is that if existence subsists in essence, it must be existent, but then its own existence raises the same question, leading to infinite regress. The author then explains that these four can be reduced to two by considering the disjunction of existence and non-existence in both the subject (essence) and the attribute (existence). The two reduced arguments: first, regarding the subject (essence): if essence is non-existent, contradiction; if existent, circularity or regress. Second, regarding the attribute (existence): if the accidental existence is non-existent, then the thing is described by its opposite; if existent, then it adds to the subject and chain of existences extends infinitely.

Key Terms
الوجود
الماهية
الزيادة
القيام
التسلسل
الدور
العارض
المعروض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, what would be the consequence of existence subsisting in essence if essence without existence is non-existent?
A) It would lead to an infinite regress.
B) It would combine existence and non-existence, which is a contradiction.
C) It would establish that existence is identical to essence.
D) It would require a third existence to mediate.

Key Takeaway
The four arguments show that adding existence to essence forces either a contradiction (if the subject lacks existence) or a circularity/regress (if the subject exists), from both the perspective of the essence and the existence itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The first argument shows that a subject cannot simultaneously combine existence and non-existence. In your own life, are there beliefs or practices where you claim one thing (e.g., trust in Allah) but act as if it were absent (e.g., worry)? Will you honestly identify one such contradiction today and seek to remove it?
?
The second argument reveals that if existence were added to essence, it would lead to an infinite regress—nothing could be truly fundamental. When you consider your own existence, do you take it as a given, or do you recognize that it points to a Necessary Being whose existence is not added? How does this affect your gratitude to Him?
?
The third and fourth arguments force us to think about self-subsistence: if existence were additional, it would not even exist itself. Does your understanding of Allah’s attributes— especially His existence—reflect this logical necessity? Or have you casually added mental constructs to His essence? Commit to studying one attribute this week with the same logical rigor.

The Reduction of the Four Arguments to Two
Reduction of four arguments to two by disjunction on subject and attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Taftazani reduces the four arguments to two by systematically considering the existence and non-existence of both the subject (essence) and the attribute (existence).

Core Idea
Four arguments collapse into two through a simple disjunction: is the subject existent or not? Is the attribute existent or not?

Having presented the four arguments, Taftazani now shows they can be reduced to just two arguments by considering the two possibilities for the subject (essence) and the two for the attribute (existence). This reduction clarifies the logical core of the dispute and shows that the identity theorists' case rests on a simple dilemma.

Guided Path
1
Read the reduction passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation of the reduction. Notice the phrase 'يرد الوجوه الأربعة إلى وجهين' – the four aspects are reduced to two aspects. Pay attention to the disjunction 'إما... أو...' (either... or...).
The reduction is based on considering existence and non-existence in the subject and the attribute.
2
Understand the two reduced arguments
The first reduced argument: regarding the subject (essence) – if it is non-existent, contradiction; if existent, circularity or regress. The second reduced argument: regarding the attribute (existence) – if it is non-existent, contradiction (thing described by its opposite); if existent, regress.
These two arguments capture all four originals without loss.
3
Compare with the four originals to see the mapping
Match each of the four original arguments to one of the two reduced arguments. For example, the first and third originals both involve the case where the subject/attribute is non-existent and lead to contradiction; the second and fourth involve the case where they are existent and lead to regress.
This shows the elegance of the reduction: it covers all possibilities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الثالث: أن وجود الشيء لو كان زائداً عليه لما كان الوجود موجوداً ضرورة امتناع تسلسل الوجودات بل معدوماً، وفيه اتصاف الشيء بنقيضه وكون ما لا ثبوت له في نفسه ثابتاً في محله. الرابع: أنه لو قام بالماهية And if it is another existence, then an infinite regress follows, except that this existence also requires the precedence of essence over it through another existence, and so on. Fourth: that if it subsists in essence, it would be existent due to the impossibility of describing a thing by its opposite and the impossibility of that which has no self-subsistence subsisting in a locus; then we shift the discussion to its existence and an infinite regress follows, because the assumption is that the existence of everything is additional to it. And the true investigation requires reducing the four aspects to two aspects by way of disjunction between existence and non-existence in both the subject and the attribute, as we mentioned in the text. First: that it subsists in essence, so the essence that is the subject is either non-existent – thus a contradiction – or existent – thus circularity or regress. Second: that the accidental existence is either non-existent – thus the thing is described by its opposite and that which has no self-subsistence subsists in a locus – or existent – thus its existence adds to it and existences regress infinitely.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage presents and analyzes arguments of those who claim that existence is identical to essence (i.e., that existence is not additional to essence) in both God and contingent beings. The author begins by explaining the proponents' core reasoning: if existence were additional to essence but not a part of it, it would have to subsist in essence like an attribute in its subject. However, this requires both the essence and the existence to be self-subsistent before the relation, which leads to a problem: either the essence serves as a locus for existence, but then existence would depend on an existing essence, causing circularity or infinite regress. Conversely, if existence itself is considered, then since existence is what makes things real, an additional existence would entail an infinite chain. The author then distills this into four explicit arguments against the addition of existence. The first argument is that if existence subsists in essence while essence is non-existent (without existence), then existence would subsist in nothing, combining existence and non-existence in one thing—a contradiction. The second argument is that for existence to subsist in essence, essence must already exist as a subject, leading either to circularity (if the existence that makes essence exist is the same as the one subsisting) or to an infinite regress (if a different existence is needed). An objection is raised against the regress argument: the regress itself is impossible, and its impossibility actually supports the opponent's claim that existence and essence are identical. The author notes a problem in this objection, pointing out that in a regress one never reaches a final totality beyond which there is no further existence. The third argument is that if existence were additional, then existence itself would not be existent (since that would need another existence, ad infinitum), so it would be non-existent—again a contradiction. The fourth argument is that if existence subsists in essence, it must be existent, but then its own existence raises the same question, leading to infinite regress. The author then explains that these four can be reduced to two by considering the disjunction of existence and non-existence in both the subject (essence) and the attribute (existence). The two reduced arguments: first, regarding the subject (essence): if essence is non-existent, contradiction; if existent, circularity or regress. Second, regarding the attribute (existence): if the accidental existence is non-existent, then the thing is described by its opposite; if existent, then it adds to the subject and chain of existences extends infinitely.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the reduction to two arguments is a simplification that ignores nuances, losing some force of the original four.
Correction: The reduction is actually a logical distillation that covers all possibilities without loss. The two arguments are more fundamental and show the core dilemma.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first of the two reduced arguments?
A) Essence cannot be a locus for existence because it would entail succession.
B) If existence subsists in essence, then essence is either non-existent (contradiction) or existent (circularity or regress).
C) Existence must be identical to essence to avoid infinite regress.
D) The impossibility of regress proves that existence is essence.

Key Takeaway
The four arguments reduce to two by a simple disjunction: either the subject (essence) or the attribute (existence) is considered, and each leads to either contradiction (if non-existent) or regress (if existent).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a complex theological debate, do you tend to multiply arguments or seek the underlying disjunction that cuts through the complexity? This lesson reduces four arguments to two – are you willing to simplify your own thinking in matters of faith, or do you resist because simplicity feels shallow?
?
The reduction hinges on whether the subject or attribute is existent or non-existent. In your understanding of God's attributes, are you clear on whether each attribute is identical to His essence or additional? Have you avoided settling this question because of its implications for your worship?
?
This argument warns against both contradiction and infinite regress. Is there any area of your belief where you rely on an endless chain of reasoning rather than accepting a foundational truth? How does that affect your certainty and your relationship with the Divine?

Reduction of the Four Arguments to Two
The author reduces the four arguments for the increase of existence over essence to two.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the four arguments against the increase of existence over essence are logically reduced to two core arguments through a disjunction between existence and non-existence.

Core Idea
Four arguments reduce to two by a simple disjunction on existence vs non-existence.

The author has presented four arguments against the view that existence adds something to essence. However, he now shows that these four can be streamlined into two fundamental dilemmas by applying a single logical technique: the disjunction between existence and non-existence to both the substratum (the essence) and the accident (existence). This reduction clarifies the core logical problem and makes the debate more manageable.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Pay attention to the terms 'معروض' (substratum) and 'عارض' (accident).
The passage is dense; read it twice.
2
Identify the two arguments
The passage presents two arguments: one focusing on the substratum (essence) and the other on the accident (existence). Locate each and note the disjunction applied.
3
Understand the disjunction method
Notice how each argument uses a binary choice: existent or non-existent. For each choice, the conclusion is either impossible (contradiction, circularity, regress) or leads to further problems.
This method is called 'ترديد' (disjunction).
4
Reflect on the significance
Think about why reducing four arguments to two is important: it streamlines the debate and focuses on essential logical issues. Consider if this reduction captures all possible objections.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتحقيق يقتضي رد الوجوه الأربعة إلى وجهين بطريق الترديد بين الوجود والعدم، في جانبي المعروض والعارض، على ما أوردنا في المتن .
الأول: أنه قام بالماهية. فالماهية المعروضة إما معدومة فيتناقض أو موجودة فيدور أو يتسلسل .
وتقرير الثاني : أن الوجود العارض إما معدوم فيتصف الشيء بنقيضه ويثبت في المحل ما لا ثبوت له في نفسه وإما موجود فيزيد وجوده عليه وتتسلسل الوجودات .
The verification requires reducing the four aspects to two ways by means of disjunction between existence and non-existence, on the two sides of the substratum and the accident, as we presented in the text. The first: that it stands in the essence. So the substratum essence is either non-existent, leading to contradiction, or existent, leading to circularity or regress. And the explanation of the second: that the accidental existence is either non-existent, then the thing is described by its opposite, and that which has no reality in itself is established in a subject; or it is existent, then its existence is added to it and the existences regress.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage reduces the four arguments against the increase of existence over essence to two core arguments by applying a disjunction between existence and non-existence to both the substratum (the essence) and the accident (existence). The author first states the principle of reduction. Then he presents the first argument: if existence is an accident inhering in the essence, then the essence must either be non-existent (impossible because it would be described by existence while non-existent, a contradiction) or existent (leading to circularity or infinite regress). The second argument: the accidental existence itself is either non-existent (then the essence is described by its opposite, and something that has no reality is predicated, impossible) or existent (then its existence would require another existence, leading to regress). These two capture the logical dilemma.

Correction: The reduction actually captures the core logical dilemma because each of the original four is a specific instance of one of these two: either the substratum or the accident is non-existent or existent. So no detail is lost.

Key Takeaway
The four arguments against increase of existence over essence reduce to two by applying a disjunction between existence and non-existence to both the substratum and the accident, each leading to impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The passage forces you to choose: either the substratum is non-existent (contradiction) or existent (regress). In your own life, are there areas where you refuse to accept the logical conclusion of a disjunction, preferring comfortable ambiguity? What would it cost you to honestly face a similar 'either-or' about your relationship with the Divine?
?
The reduction to two arguments shows that the increase of existence over essence is rationally impossible. If this is true, then your very existence is not an added property to a prior essence—you are not a pre-existing self that 'receives' existence. How does this challenge your daily sense of self? Will you live today as if your existence is a direct gift from the Necessary Being, not a mere addition to an already-existing 'you'?

The Summary Answer: Existence is Mentally Added to Essence
Author gives a summary answer: existence is mentally added.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's summary response to the first argument: that the increase and inherence of existence in essence are purely mental, not external.

Core Idea
Existence is mentally added to essence, not externally.

This lesson presents the concise summary answer. Before diving into detailed refutations, the author states the core principle: the extra existence is only a mental consideration. This sets the stage for the more complex arguments to follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the summary claim.
Pay attention to the phrase 'بحسب العقل'.
2
Identify the key phrase
Notice 'اختصاص ناعت' (predicative specification) and how existence is considered a meaning with respect to essence.
3
Contrast with external inherence
The author distinguishes mental inherence from external inherence like whiteness in body. Understand why external inherence would lead to impossibilities.
External inherence would require real separation, which is problematic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : إما إجمالاً فهو أن زيادة الوجود على الماهية وقيامه بها إنما هو بحسب العقل، بأن يلاحظ كلاً منهما من غير ملاحظة الآخر، ويعتبر الوجود معنى له اختصاص ناعت بالماهية لا بحسب الخارج بأن يقوم الوجود بالماهية قيام البياض بالجسم، وتلزم المحالات، وإما تفصيلاً.
فعن الأول : أن قيامه بالماهية من حيث هي هي لا بالماهية المعدومة ليلزم التناقض ، ولا بالماهية الموجودة ليلزم الدور أو التسلسل .
فإن قيل : إن أريد بالماهية من حيث هي هي ما لا يكون الوجود أو العدم نفسها ولا جزءاً منها على ما قيل فغير مفيد لأن العروض كاف في لزوم المحالات، وإن أريد ما لا يكون موجوداً ولا معدوماً لا بالعروض ولا بغيره، فالتناقض فيه أظهر لأن اللاوجود نقيض الوجود بلا نزاع ولا اشتباه .
قلنا : المراد ما لا يعتبر فيه الوجود ولا العدم، وإن كان لا ينفك عن أحدهما في الخارج. فإن قيل : عدم الانفكاك عن أحدهما كاف في لزوم المحال، لأنه إن قارن العدم فيناقض، أو الوجود فيدور أو يتسلسل .
قلنا : قيام الوجود بالماهية أمر عقلي ليس كقيام البياض بالجسم ليلزم تقدمها عليه بالوجود، تقدماً ذاتياً أو زمانياً، فتلزم المحالات، بل غاية الأمر أنه يلزم تقدمها عليه بالوجود العقلي، ولا استحالة فيه لجواز أن تلاحظ وحدها من غير ملاحظة وجود خارجي أو ذهني، ويكون لها وجود ذهني لا بملاحظة العقل، فإن عدم الاعتبار غير اعتبار العدم، وإن اعتبر العقل وجودها الذهني لم يلزم التسلسل، بل ينقطع بانقطاع الاعتبار.
وأما القائلون بنفي الوجود الذهني فجوابهم الاقتصار على منع لزوم تقدم
And the answer: either in summary, it is that the increase of existence over the essence and its standing in it is only according to the mind, such that each is observed without observing the other, and existence is considered a meaning having a predicative specification with respect to the essence, not externally such that existence stands in the essence like whiteness stands in the body, leading to impossibilities; or in detail. As for the first: that its standing in the essence from the perspective of the essence itself is not in the non-existent essence so that contradiction would follow, nor in the existent essence so that circularity or regress would follow. For if it is said: if by 'essence from the perspective of the essence itself' is meant that existence or non-existence is neither identical to it nor a part of it, as is said, then it is not useful because inherence alone is sufficient for the impossibilities; and if it is meant that which is neither existent nor non-existent, neither by inherence nor otherwise, then the contradiction is more apparent because non-existence is the opposite of existence without dispute or confusion. We say: what is meant is that in which existence nor non-existence is considered, even if it does not separate from one of them in the external. For if it is said: the lack of separation from one of them is sufficient for the impossibility, because if it is accompanied by non-existence it contradicts, or by existence then circularity or regress. We say: the standing of existence in the essence is a mental matter, not like the standing of whiteness in the body so that its priority over it in existence, either essential or temporal priority, would be necessary, thus leading to impossibilities. Rather, all that follows is that it is necessary for it to have priority over it in mental existence, and there is no impossibility in that because it is permissible that it be observed alone without observing external or mental existence, and it has mental existence not by the mind's observation. Indeed, non-consideration is not the same as considering non-existence. And if the mind considers its mental existence, regress does not follow, but rather it is cut off by the cutting off of consideration. As for those who deny mental existence, their answer is limited to preventing the necessity of priority...
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage provides the response to the objections. First, a summary answer: the increase and inherence are only mental, not external. Then a detailed answer for the first argument: existence stands in the essence from the perspective of the essence itself, not as inhering in an already non-existent or already existent essence. An objection follows: what does 'essence itself' mean? If it means essence without existence or non-existence as constituents, that is still problematic because inherence alone implies the essence must be either existent or non-existent. The author responds: the essence considered in itself neither includes existence nor non-existence, though it cannot be separated from one in reality. The priority involved is only mental, not temporal or essential, so regress is avoided. Mental consideration cuts off the regress, and non-consideration is not the same as considering non-existence. Finally, for those who deny mental existence altogether, the answer is simply to reject the necessity of priority, but the text is cut off.

Key Terms
بحسب العقل
اختصاص ناعت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the existence-essence distinction is real and external, like two separate things.
Correction: This corrects that it is only mental, not a real external separation.

Checkpoint
According to the summary answer, where does existence inhere in the essence?
A) In the external world
B) Only in the mind
C) As a physical property
D) Both externally and mentally

Key Takeaway
Existence is only mentally considered to inhere in essence, not externally.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your existence is not an external property you possess but a mental consideration of your essence, what does that reveal about your absolute dependence on the One whose existence is His essence? Do you truly live as if your very being is given to you at every moment? How will you remind yourself of this dependency today?
?
The author warns that if existence were an external accident like whiteness, absurdities would follow. Reflect on the 'absurdities' in your own life when you treat your existence as something you own and control. In what specific situation do you need to acknowledge that you are not the source of your own being? What concrete act of humility can you perform to affirm that your existence is a gift?
?
Consider the phrase 'بحسب العقل' — according to the mind. Your mind can separate existence from essence, but in reality they are not separate. How does this mental exercise change the way you view your own identity? Are you willing to let go of the illusion that you exist independently from God? What is holding you back from that surrender?

Detailed Refutation: Existence Inheres in Essence as Such
Author provides a detailed refutation of the first argument.

Existence inheres in essence as such, not in a pre-existing subject.

This lesson delves into the detailed refutation of the first argument. The author explains that existence stands in the essence from the perspective of the essence itself, avoiding the pitfalls of circularity or regress. The discussion addresses objections about the meaning of 'essence itself' and the nature of priority.
