Mental Existence vs. Real Existence: Answering Three Objections
Al-Taftazani presents three objections and their answer regarding mental existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the crucial distinction between real existence (al-wujud al-muta'asil) and mental existence, and how it resolves the apparent absurdities of conceiving heat, the heavens, and non-existents.

Core Idea
Mental forms are not real essences—they are abstract images.

Al-Taftazani addresses a common philosophical objection: if conceiving something means it exists in the mind, then absurd consequences follow—like the mind becoming hot when conceiving heat. He shows that this confusion arises from equating mental existence (a mere form) with real existence (the essence). Let's examine the objections and his precise response.

Guided Path
1
Read the three objections
Quickly read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the three objections: heat/cold, heavens, non-existents.
The objections are based on the assumption that conception entails actual existence in the mind.
2
Understand the problem in each objection
For each objection, ask: what absurdity follows if we accept that conception implies mental existence? Notice the contradiction or impossibility claimed.
The first two involve material properties, the third involves external existence.
3
Grasp the key distinction
Focus on the answer: the distinction between real existence (constituting the essence) and non-real existence (the mental form). See how this distinction is applied to each objection.
Heat's essence vs. its image; heavens' essence vs. mental picture; non-existents as mental forms, not real essences.
4
Connect the answer to all objections
See how the same distinction resolves all three. Then read the concluding summary about quiddity and essence correspondence.
The final paragraph ties it together: the mental form is universal and abstract, while the external essence is particular and the source of potentiality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : لو كان تصور الشيء مستلزماً لحصوله في العقل، لزم من تصور الحرارة والبرودة أن يكون الذهن حاراً بارداً وهو محال، لما فيه من اجتماع الضدين، واتصاف العقل بما هو من خواص الأجسام . الثاني : : أنه يلزم أن تحصل السموات بعضها في العقل عند تعقلها في (1) الكل، وفي الخيال عند تخيلها، وهو باطل بالضرورة. الثالث: أنه يلزم من تعقل المعدومات وجودها في الخارج، لكونها موجودة في العقل الموجود في الخارج ، مع القطع بأن الموجود في الموجود في الشيء موجود في ذلك الشيء، كالماء الموجود في الكوز الموجود في البيت. والجواب : أن مبنى الكل على عدم التفرقة بين الوجود المتأصل الذي بـه الهوية العينية، وغير المتأصل الذي به الصورة العقلية فإن المتصف بالحرارة ما تقوم به هوية الحرارة لا صورتها، والتضاد إنما هو بين هويتي الحرارة والبرودة لا بين صورتيهما (۲) والذي علم بالضرورة استحالة حصوله في العقل والخيال هو هويات السموات لا صورها الكلية أو الجزئية (۳) ، والموجود في الموجود في الشيء إنما يكون موجوداً في ذلك الشيء، إذا كان الوجودان متأصلين، ويكون الموجودان هويتين كوجود الماء في الكوز والكوز في البيت، بخلاف وجود المعدوم في الذهن الموجود في الخارج. فإن الحاصل في الذهن من المعدوم صورة، والوجود غير متأصل، ومن الذهن في الخارج هوية. والوجود متأصل . وبالجملة فماهية الشيء أعني صورته العقلية مخالفة لهويته العينية في كثير من اللوازم فإن الأولى : كلية ومجردة بخلاف الثانية، والثانية : مبدأ للإمكان (٤) بخلاف الأولى ومعنى المطابقة بينهما : أن الماهية إذا وجدت في الخارج كانت تلك الهوية والهوية إذا جردت عن العوارض المشخصة واللواحق الغريبة، كانت تلك الماهية فلا يرد ما يقال إن الصورة العقلية إن ساوت (*) الصورة الخارجية لزمت المحالات وإلا لم تكن صورة لها .
First: If conception of a thing necessitated its obtaining in the mind, then from conceiving heat and cold it would follow that the mind is hot and cold, which is impossible, because it involves the conjunction of two opposites, and the attribution to the mind of what is among the properties of bodies. Second: It would follow that the heavens partially obtain in the mind when they are conceived as a whole, and in the imagination when they are imagined, and that is evidently false. Third: It would follow from conceiving non-existents that they exist externally, because they exist in the mind which exists externally, while being certain that what exists in something existing in a thing exists in that thing, like water existing in a jar existing in the house. The answer: The basis of all [these objections] is the failure to distinguish between the real existence by which the external essence subsists, and the non-real [existence] by which the mental form subsists. For what is qualified by heat is that in which the essence of heat subsists, not its form. Contradiction is only between the essences of heat and cold, not between their forms. What is known with necessity to be impossible to obtain in the mind and imagination is the essences of the heavens, not their universal or particular forms. Something existing in something existing in a thing is only existent in that thing if the two existences are real, and the two existents are essences, like the existence of water in the jar and the jar in the house; unlike the existence of a non-existent in the mind which exists externally. For what obtains in the mind from a non-existent is a form, and the existence is non-real, while [what obtains] from the mind in the external is an essence, and the existence is real. In sum, the quiddity of a thing, meaning its mental form, differs from its external essence in many properties. For the first is universal and abstract, unlike the second. The second is the source of potentiality, unlike the first. The meaning of correspondence between them is that when the quiddity exists externally, it is that essence, and when the essence is stripped of individuating accidents and extraneous attachments, it is that quiddity. Thus what is said is not considered: that if the mental form equals the external form, impossibilities follow; otherwise it is not a form of it.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage presents three objections against the view that conception (tasawwur) of something necessitates its existence in the mind, followed by a response. The author first lists the objections: first, if conception of heat and cold required their existence in the mind, then the mind would be hot and cold — impossible due to contradiction and attributing bodily properties to the mind. Second, then the heavens would exist in the mind when conceived as a whole, which is false. Third, then non-existents would exist externally because they are in the mind. The answer: the confusion is due to failing to distinguish between real existence (al-wujud al-muta'asil) which constitutes the essence, and unreal existence (al-wujud ghayr al-muta'asil) which is the mental form. Heat's essence (huwiyya) is what is qualified by heat, not its mental image. Contradiction only occurs between essences, not between mental images. What is known to be impossible to obtain in the mind is the essences of the heavens, not their universal or particular images. For something existing in something else to be existent in that thing, both existences must be real, like water in a jar. But the existence of a non-existent in the mind is a mental form, not real. Therefore, the quiddity (mahiyya) or mental form differs from the external essence in many properties. The mental form is universal and abstract, while the external essence is particular and a source of potentiality. Correspondence between them means that when the quiddity exists externally, it is that essence, and when the essence is stripped of individuating accidents, it is that quiddity. Thus the objection that if the mental image equals the external image then impossibilities follow, otherwise it is not an image of it, is rejected.

Key Terms
تصور (tasawwur)
وجود متأصل (wujud muta'asil)
صورة عقلية (sura 'aqliyya)
هوية عينية (huwiyya 'ayniyya)
ماهية (mahiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That mental existence is the same as real existence, leading to the idea that conceiving something makes it actually present in the mind with its material properties.
Correction: Mental existence is a non-real existence (wujud ghayr muta'asil) that carries only the form (sura) of the thing, not its essence (huwiyya). Thus, conceiving heat does not make the mind hot; conceiving the heavens does not place their matter in the mind; and conceiving a non-existent does not bring it into external existence.

Checkpoint
According to the answer in this passage, what is the key distinction that resolves the three objections?
A) The difference between the mind and the external world
B) The difference between real existence (al-wujud al-muta'asil) and non-real existence (al-wujud ghayr al-muta'asil)
C) The difference between conception and imagination
D) The difference between universal and particular forms

Key Takeaway
The difference between real existence (huwiyya 'ayniyya) and mental form (sura 'aqliyya) is essential to avoid contradictions about mental existence—conception yields only an abstract image, not the concrete essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You conceive of your own intentions and actions daily. How often do you mistake your mental image of a good deed—your intention—for the real deed itself? Will you tonight check whether your mental form of worship matches its external reality before Allah?
?
Al-Taftazani says that conceiving a non-existent does not give it real existence. Yet you may spend hours mentally 'existing' in worries or fantasies. What is one such mental form you will deliberately distinguish from reality starting tomorrow, and what action will you take to confirm that distinction?
?
The passage teaches that correspondence between mental form and external essence requires stripping away accidental qualities. When you reflect on your own identity, do you conflate your accidental traits (wealth, status, failures) with your essence as a servant of Allah? What would change if you separated your mental self-image from your real essence in the sight of your Creator?

The Four Positions on Non-Existence and the Correct View
Al-Taftazani enumerates four positions on non-existence and intermediate, and argues for the correct view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four possible positions on whether the non-existent is established and whether there is an intermediate between existence and non-existence, and the proof for the correct view.

Core Idea
Non-existence has no reality, and there is no middle state between existence and non-existence.

Al-Taftazani begins by outlining the disagreements among scholars about the status of non-existent entities and whether a third state exists. He maps out four logical possibilities and then argues that only one is correct based on the synonymy of existence with establishment and non-existence with negation. This lesson guides you through these positions and the reasoning behind the chosen view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the four doctrines
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: the four possibilities are derived from two binary questions.
The passage uses a logical enumeration style common in kalam.
2
Identify each of the four positions
List the four positions as described: affirming both, denying both, affirming first and denying second, or the opposite. Understand what 'first' and 'second' refer to.
The 'first' is whether the non-existent is established; the 'second' is whether there is an intermediate.
3
Understand the proof for the correct view
The truth is to deny both. The proof is based on the synonymy: existence = establishment, non-existence = negation. Since negation is not established, non-existence is not established; since there is no intermediate between established and negated, there is none between existent and non-existent.
This argument is concise and foundational.
4
Reflect on the implications for kalam
Consider why this matters: if non-existence had reality or if there were an intermediate state, theological reasoning about creation, attributes, etc., would change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حسب الاحتمالات قد اختلفوا في (1) أن المعدوم هل هو ثابت وشيء أم لا؟ وفي أنه هل بين الموجود والمعدوم واسطة أم لا ؟ والمذاهب أربعة (2) أعني إثبات الأمرين أو نفيهما، أو إثبات الأول ونفي الثاني أو بالعكس. وذلك أنه إما أن يكون المعدوم ثابتاً أو لا. وعلى التقديرين إما أن يكون بين الموجود والمعدوم واسطة أو لا . والحق نفيهما بناء على أن الوجود يرادف الثبوت، والعدم يرادف النفي، فكما أن النفي ليس ثابتاً، فكذا المعدوم، وكما أنه لا واسطة بين الثابت والمنفي، فكذا بين الموجود والمعدوم، وأما الشيئية فتساوق الوجود بمعنى أن كل موجود شيء، وبالعكس، ولفظ المساوقة يستعمل عندهم فيما يعم الاتحاد في المفهوم فيكون اللفظان مترادفين والمساواة في الصدق فيكونان متباينين ولهم تردد في اتحاد مفهوم الوجود والشيئية. بل ربما يدعي نفيه بناء على أن قولنا : السواد موجود، يفيد فائدة يعتد بها بخلاف قولنا : السواد شيء فصار الحاصل أن كل ما يمكن أن يعلم إن كان له تحقق في الخارج أو الذهن فموجود وثابت وشيء وإلا فمعدوم، ومنفي ولا شيء
According to the possibilities, they disagreed concerning whether the non-existent is established and a thing or not, and concerning whether there is an intermediate between the existent and the non-existent or not. There are four doctrines: affirming both matters, denying both, affirming the first and denying the second, or the opposite. That is because either the non-existent is established or not, and in both cases either there is an intermediate between the existent and the non-existent or not. The truth is to deny both, based on the fact that existence is synonymous with establishment and non-existence is synonymous with negation. So just as negation is not established, so also the non-existent, and just as there is no intermediate between the established and the negated, so also between the existent and the non-existent. As for thingness, it is commensurate with existence in the sense that every existent is a thing, and vice versa. The term 'commensuration' is used by them to include both unity in concept so that the two terms are synonymous, and equality in truth so that they are distinct. They have hesitation concerning the unity of the concept of existence and thingness. Rather, one might claim its negation based on the fact that our saying 'blackness exists' conveys a worthwhile benefit unlike our saying 'blackness is a thing.' So the conclusion is that everything that can be known—if it has realization externally or mentally—is existent, established, and a thing; otherwise it is non-existent, negated, and nothing.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof of the correct position on the status of non-existence and whether there is an intermediate between existence and non-existence. The author begins by stating the disagreement: does the non-existent have any reality or thingness? And is there a middle state? He then notes there are four logical possibilities. The correct view is to deny both: non-existence is not established and there is no intermediate. The proof is based on the synonymy of existence with establishment and non-existence with negation. Just as negation has no reality, so non-existence has none. Then he discusses thingness and its relation to existence. He explains that 'thingness' is commensurate with existence, meaning everything existent is a thing and vice versa. He elaborates on the term 'commensuration', noting it can refer to either synonymy (if the concepts are identical) or co-extensiveness (if the concepts are distinct but always apply together). Scholars hesitated on whether the concepts of existence and thingness are identical. Some argued they are not, because saying 'blackness exists' is informative while 'blackness is a thing' is trivial. The final conclusion: anything real in the mind or external is existent, established, and a thing; otherwise it is non-existent, negated, and nothing.

Key Terms
المعدوم
الثبوت
الوجود
العدم
النفي
الواسطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the non-existent has some kind of reality or that there is a third state between existence and non-existence.
Correction: The passage argues that non-existence is pure negation with no establishment, and there is no intermediate because existence and non-existence are mutually exclusive and exhaustive.

Checkpoint
According to the correct view in this passage, which of the following is true?
A) The non-existent is established and there is an intermediate.
B) The non-existent is not established and there is an intermediate.
C) The non-existent is established and there is no intermediate.
D) The non-existent is not established and there is no intermediate.

Key Takeaway
Non-existence cannot be considered established, and there is no intermediate state between being and non-being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever treat something as 'real' or 'significant' in your life that you know is actually non-existent? How does this passage challenge you to realign your thinking with the truth that non-existence is nothing?
?
The passage argues that there is no middle ground between existence and non-existence. Is there an area in your life where you are trying to maintain a compromise between faith and denial? What step will you take today to move fully into the truth of existence as established in Allah?
?
If existence is the only reality, how should that change your priorities and attachments? What is one worldly attachment that you will consciously recognize as non-existent in significance, and how will you act on that recognition today?

Thingness and Its Relationship to Existence
Al-Taftazani discusses thingness and its relation to existence, including the debate on conceptual identity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of thingness, its relation to existence, and the debate over whether they are identical in concept.

Core Idea
Thingness and existence are commensurate: every existent is a thing and every thing is existent, even if their exact conceptual relationship is debated.

After establishing the correct view on non-existence, al-Taftazani turns to the concept of 'thingness' (shay’iyya). He explains that it is commensurate with existence, but scholars disagree on whether the two terms are synonymous or merely co-extensive. This lesson unpacks that discussion and its implications for theological language.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on thingness
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that the discussion on thingness builds on the previous conclusion about non-existence.
2
Understand the definition of commensuration
Commensuration (musāwaqa) means that every existent is a thing and every thing is existent. That is the basic claim.
This is a relation of co-extension.
3
Note the two interpretations of commensuration
Scholars use the term 'commensuration' to cover two possibilities: synonymy (the two terms share the same meaning) or co-extensiveness (they refer to the same objects but have different meanings).
4
Understand the hesitation on conceptual unity
Al-Taftazani notes that there is hesitation (taraddud) on whether existence and thingness are identical in concept. Some argue they are not, because saying 'blackness exists' is informative while 'blackness is a thing' is trivial.
This example distinguishes the two terms.
5
Grasp the final conclusion
The conclusion is that anything real—whether in the mind or externally—is existent, established, and a thing; otherwise it is non-existent, negated, and nothing.
This ties back to the earlier discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حسب الاحتمالات قد اختلفوا في (1) أن المعدوم هل هو ثابت وشيء أم لا؟ وفي أنه هل بين الموجود والمعدوم واسطة أم لا ؟ والمذاهب أربعة (2) أعني إثبات الأمرين أو نفيهما، أو إثبات الأول ونفي الثاني أو بالعكس. وذلك أنه إما أن يكون المعدوم ثابتاً أو لا. وعلى التقديرين إما أن يكون بين الموجود والمعدوم واسطة أو لا . والحق نفيهما بناء على أن الوجود يرادف الثبوت، والعدم يرادف النفي، فكما أن النفي ليس ثابتاً، فكذا المعدوم، وكما أنه لا واسطة بين الثابت والمنفي، فكذا بين الموجود والمعدوم، وأما الشيئية فتساوق الوجود بمعنى أن كل موجود شيء، وبالعكس، ولفظ المساوقة يستعمل عندهم فيما يعم الاتحاد في المفهوم فيكون اللفظان مترادفين والمساواة في الصدق فيكونان متباينين ولهم تردد في اتحاد مفهوم الوجود والشيئية. بل ربما يدعي نفيه بناء على أن قولنا : السواد موجود، يفيد فائدة يعتد بها بخلاف قولنا : السواد شيء فصار الحاصل أن كل ما يمكن أن يعلم إن كان له تحقق في الخارج أو الذهن فموجود وثابت وشيء وإلا فمعدوم، ومنفي ولا شيء
According to the possibilities, they disagreed concerning whether the non-existent is established and a thing or not, and concerning whether there is an intermediate between the existent and the non-existent or not. There are four doctrines: affirming both matters, denying both, affirming the first and denying the second, or the opposite. That is because either the non-existent is established or not, and in both cases either there is an intermediate between the existent and the non-existent or not. The truth is to deny both, based on the fact that existence is synonymous with establishment and non-existence is synonymous with negation. So just as negation is not established, so also the non-existent, and just as there is no intermediate between the established and the negated, so also between the existent and the non-existent. As for thingness, it is commensurate with existence in the sense that every existent is a thing, and vice versa. The term 'commensuration' is used by them to include both unity in concept so that the two terms are synonymous, and equality in truth so that they are distinct. They have hesitation concerning the unity of the concept of existence and thingness. Rather, one might claim its negation based on the fact that our saying 'blackness exists' conveys a worthwhile benefit unlike our saying 'blackness is a thing.' So the conclusion is that everything that can be known—if it has realization externally or mentally—is existent, established, and a thing; otherwise it is non-existent, negated, and nothing.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof of the correct position on the status of non-existence and whether there is an intermediate between existence and non-existence. The author begins by stating the disagreement: does the non-existent have any reality or thingness? And is there a middle state? He then notes there are four logical possibilities. The correct view is to deny both: non-existence is not established and there is no intermediate. The proof is based on the synonymy of existence with establishment and non-existence with negation. Just as negation has no reality, so non-existence has none. Then he discusses thingness and its relation to existence. He explains that 'thingness' is commensurate with existence, meaning everything existent is a thing and vice versa. He elaborates on the term 'commensuration', noting it can refer to either synonymy (if the concepts are identical) or co-extensiveness (if the concepts are distinct but always apply together). Scholars hesitated on whether the concepts of existence and thingness are identical. Some argued they are not, because saying 'blackness exists' is informative while 'blackness is a thing' is trivial. The final conclusion: anything real in the mind or external is existent, established, and a thing; otherwise it is non-existent, negated, and nothing.

المساوقة
الاتحاد في المفهوم

Correction: The passage shows there is a debate; they are co-extensive but may differ in concept, as the blackness example illustrates.

Checkpoint

B) Existence and thingness are synonymous.
C) Saying 'blackness exists' is informative while 'blackness is a thing' is trivial, suggesting the concepts might differ.
D) Blackness exists only in the mind.

Key Takeaway
Thingness coincides with existence in extension, but their conceptual identity is subject to scholarly disagreement.

First Proof for the State: Existence and Its Nature
Taftazani presents the first proof for the state based on the logical classification of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof for the existence of the 'haal' (state) based on the logical classification of existence, including the objection and response.

Core Idea
Existence cannot be simply 'existent' or 'non-existent' without contradiction.

In this passage, Taftazani introduces the first proof for the state (haal) by examining the logical status of existence itself. The proof argues that existence cannot be classified as 'existent' or 'non-existent' without leading to absurdities, which opens the door for an intermediate category—the state. Let's explore the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the logical structure: a claim, an objection, and a response.
Pay attention to the term 'تسلسل' (infinite regress) and 'نقيض' (opposite).
2
Identify the initial premise
The proof starts by stating that existence is not existent (otherwise infinite regress) and not non-existent (otherwise it would be characterized by its opposite). Why is this a problem?
3
Examine the objection
The objector claims that existence does not fall under the division into existent and non-existent. The author responds that this itself is an admission of an intermediate state (al-wasita).
4
Analyze the response (qulna)
The response offers two possibilities: existence is identical to the existent, or it is non-existent. It refines the terms to avoid the contradiction of being characterized by its opposite.
The key is that the problem only arises if existence were non-existence or the existent were non-existent.
5
Connect to the state (haal)
Understand how this argument supports the existence of a state as a third category between existent and non-existent, which is the goal of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: احتج المثبتون للحال بوجوده : الأول: أن الوجود ليس بموجود وإلا تسلسل ولا معدوم وإلا اتصف بنقيضه، والقول بأنه لا يرد عليه القسمة اعتراف بالواسطة . قلنا : موجود ووجوده عينه، أو معدوم وإنما يلزم الاتصاف بالنقيض، لو كان الوجود عدماً أو الموجد معدماً .
He said: The affirmers of the state (al-haal) adduced proof for its existence: First: that existence (al-wujud) is not existent, otherwise there would be infinite regress, nor non-existent, otherwise it would be characterized by its opposite. And the claim that it does not fall under the division is an admission of an intermediate [state]. We say: It is existent and its existence is identical to it, or it is non-existent; indeed, the characterization by the opposite would only follow if existence were non-existence or the existent were non-existent.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof for the existence of the 'haal' (state), a central concept in kalam that refers to a property that is neither fully existent nor non-existent. The proof runs as follows: Existence (al-wujud) cannot be classified as existent (mawjud), because if it were existent, it would need another existence, leading to infinite regress. It also cannot be classified as non-existent (ma'dum), because if it were non-existent, it would be characterized by its opposite, i.e., non-existence, which contradicts the fact that existence is a property of existent things. The opponent might say that existence does not fall under the division into existent and non-existent, but that is an admission that there is an intermediate category (al-wasita), which is precisely the 'haal' (state). The author then provides the response (qulna): Existence is either identical to the existent (i.e., it is the same as the existent's being existent) or it is non-existent. The problem of being characterized by the opposite only arises if existence were non-existence or the existent were non-existent. But if we say existence is identical to the existent, there is no separate property; if we say it is non-existent, the issue disappears because the existent is not non-existent. The response refines the terms of the argument.

Key Terms
حال
وجود
موجود
معدوم
تسلسل
نقيض
واسطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument proves that existence is a separate entity distinct from the existent.
Correction: The argument is about the logical category of existence, not its ontological separation. The state is a property, not an independent entity.

Checkpoint
What is the initial premise of the first proof for the state?
A) Existence is always identical to the existent.
B) Existence is not existent, otherwise there would be infinite regress.
C) Existence is non-existent, otherwise it would be self-contradictory.
D) The state is a third category between existent and non-existent.

Key Takeaway
The first proof for the state uses the logical impossibility of classifying existence as either existent or non-existent to argue for an intermediate category.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You take your own existence for granted every moment. But this argument demands you ask: is your existence simply a fact, or is there a deeper structure to it? How does realizing that existence is not a simple binary category change the way you think about your own being and the One who gave it to you?
?
When you say 'I am', do you mean that you are a thing that exists, or that existence itself is you? This distinction matters for your relationship with Allah, Who is Existence itself. How does this proof challenge you to refine your understanding of your own existence in relation to His?
?
If existence is neither existent nor non-existent, then where do you stand? Are you willing to dwell in that logical tension, or will you dismiss it? Let this argument press you to admit that your own categories are insufficient—and to humbly accept that Allah's reality transcends all divisions.

Second Proof for the State: The Universal and Its Particulars
Taftazani presents the second proof for the state based on the universal's impossibility of being existent or non-existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof for the existence of the state (hal), which argues that the universal is neither existent nor non-existent externally, and that universals and genera are only mental distinctions.

Core Idea
The universal is neither existent nor non-existent in external reality; it is a mental consideration.

Taftazani presents a second proof for the state (hal) by examining the nature of the universal (al-kulli). The argument shows that the universal cannot be either existent or non-existent in the external world, leading to the conclusion that universals and genera are mental distinctions. This lesson unpacks the proof and its response, clarifying the relationship between universals, particulars, and mind-dependent reality.

Guided Path
1
Understand the core claim
Read the first part of the passage: 'The universal is not existent... nor non-existent'. Consider why each option is impossible for a universal.
The universal would be particularized if existent, and would not be part of the existent if non-existent.
2
Extend to genus and differentia
The same reasoning applies to genus (jins) and differentia (fasl) with respect to species (naw'). Notice the implication for accidents: if the universal were existent, it would cause an accident to subsist in an accident.
3
Examine the response
Read the response starting with 'We say: There is no composition in external existence...'. Understand the examples of human/particularity of Zayd and color/light-absorbing.
The response denies external composition; all distinctions are mental.
4
Connect to the concept of state
Reflect on how this proof supports the doctrine of states: universals like 'human' are states that are neither existent nor non-existent externally, but are real mental considerations.
The state is a mental attribute with no external existence yet not non-existent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: الكلي ليس بموجود، وإلا لكان مشخصاً ولا معدوم وإلا لما كان جزءاً للموجود، وكذلك (٣) حال كل جنس أو فصل مع نوعه، على أنه لو وجد يلزم في الأعراض قيام العرض بالعرض. قلنا : لا تركيب في الخارج، إذ ليس هنا شيء هو إنسان وآخر خصوصية زيد ولا في السواد شيء هو لون وآخر قابض للبصر، وآخر مركب منهما يقوم واحد منهما بآخر، على أن مثل هذا القيام ليس من قيام العرض بالمحل في شيء، وإنما التمايز في الذهن، فثبت فيه الكلي والجنس .
Second: The universal (al-kulli) is not existent, otherwise it would be particularized; nor non-existent, otherwise it would not be a part of the existent. Similarly is the state of every genus (jins) or differentia (fasl) with its species (naw'), such that if it existed, there would follow, in the case of accidents, the subsistence of an accident in an accident. We say: There is no composition in external existence, for there is not here something that is 'human' and another that is the particularity of Zayd, nor in blackness is there something that is 'color' and another that is 'light-absorbing', and another composed of them such that one subsists in the other. Moreover, this kind of subsistence is not at all like the subsistence of an accident in a substrate. Rather, the distinction is in the mind, so the universal and the genus are established therein.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for the existence of the state, focusing on the universal (al-kulli). The argument begins: The universal (e.g., 'human') is not existent, because if it were existent, it would be a particular (mushakhkhas) with specific characteristics, which contradicts its universality. It is also not non-existent, because if it were non-existent, it could not be a part of the existent (e.g., the species is part of the existent individual). The same reasoning applies to every genus (jins) or differentia (fasl) with respect to its species (naw'). Additionally, if the universal were existent, it would cause an accidental entity to subsist in another accidental entity (qiyam al-'arad bi al-'arad), which is problematic in kalam. The response (qulna) denies that there is any composition in external existence. For example, in a human being, there is not a separate entity 'human' and a separate entity 'the particularity of Zayd'; similarly, in blackness, there is not a separate entity 'color' and another 'light-absorbing' and a third composed of them. The subsistence of one in the other is not the usual subsistence of accident in substance. Rather, all distinctions (tamayyuz) are only in the mind. Therefore, the universal and genus are established in the mind, not as external realities. This supports the concept of the state as a mental consideration.

معدوم

قيام العرض بالعرض
تركيب
ذهن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Universals like 'humanity' have real external existence as separate entities.
Correction: The passage argues that universals are neither existent nor non-existent externally; they are mental distinctions. There is no composition of universal and particular in the external world.

Checkpoint
What does the second proof claim would happen if the universal were existent?
A) It would be particularized (mushakhkhas).
B) It would cause an infinite regress.
C) It would be non-existent.
D) It would be a genus.

Key Takeaway
The universal is neither existent nor non-existent in external reality; it is a mental consideration, which supports the doctrine of states (ahwal).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The passage teaches that distinctions like 'human' and 'particularity of Zayd' are only mental, not external. How might this understanding change the way you view your worth before Allah? Commit to seeing yourself as a unified creation of Allah, not a collection of parts.

If universals have no external reality, what does that mean for your understanding of Allah's attributes? Are you tempted to think of His mercy or power as separate 'parts' of Him? How does this lesson guard you against that error? Resolve to worship Allah as one indivisible Being whose attributes are not separate existences.

Second Argument for al-Ḥāl: Universals and the Reply
Taftazani gives the second argument using universals and replies that composition is mental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument for the state (al-ḥāl) based on universals and Taftazani's refutation that composition and differentiation are mental, not external.

Core Idea
Universals are not external existents but mental distinctions.

This section continues the debate over the existence of states (al-ḥāl). The second argument claims that universals must be intermediate states because they are neither fully existent nor non-existent. Taftazani responds by showing that all composition and differentiation occur in the mind, not in external reality. Let's examine the argument and its rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow of argument and reply.
Notice how the argument is presented concisely and then answered.
2
Identify the argument's structure
The argument says the universal is neither existent nor non-existent, and if it were existent it would cause accidents to subsist in accidents.
3
Identify the reply's key points
The reply denies external composition, gives examples (human/Zayd, color/blackness), and asserts that distinctions are mental.
Focus on the examples to understand the reply.
4
Reflect on the implication for al-ḥāl
Ask yourself: does this argument and reply support or weaken the existence of al-ḥāl? The reply shows no need for an intermediate state.
This connects to the broader debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: الكلي ليس بموجود، وإلا لكان مشخصاً ولا معدوم وإلا لما كان جزءاً للموجود، وكذلك حال كل جنس أو فصل مع نوعه، على أنه لو وجد يلزم في الأعراض قيام العرض بالعرض.
قلنا : لا تركيب في الخارج، إذ ليس هنا شيء هو إنسان وآخر خصوصية زيد ولا في السواد شيء هو لون وآخر قابض للبصر، وآخر مركب منهما يقوم واحد منهما بآخر، على أن مثل هذا القيام ليس من قيام العرض بالمحل في شيء، وإنما التمايز في الذهن، فثبت فيه الكلي والجنس .
Second: The universal (al-kullī) is not existent, otherwise it would be individuated, nor non-existent, otherwise it would not be a part of the existent. And similarly the case of every genus or differentia with its species, such that if it existed, then in accidents there would be an accident subsisting in an accident. We said: There is no composition in the external world, for there is nothing here that is a human and another that is Zayd's particularity, nor in blackness is there something that is color and another that is a constrictor of sight, and another composed from them such that one subsists in another. Moreover, such subsistence is not like the subsistence of an accident in a substrate at all. Rather, differentiation is in the mind, so the universal and genus are established in it [the mind].
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument for the existence of the state (al-ḥāl) and the reply. The argument is based on the universal (al-kullī). The universal is not existent as a concrete individual, otherwise it would be individuated and lose its universality, and it is not non-existent because it is part of existent things (e.g., humanity is part of Zayd). Similarly, genus and differentia with their species would be problematic if they had existence, because in accidents it would lead to an accident subsisting in another accident. The author replies that there is no external composition: in external reality, there is no separate entity of 'human' and another of 'Zayd's particularity' — the particular is the individual itself. Similarly, in blackness, there is no separate entity of 'color' and 'constrictor of sight' — these are conceptual distinctions. The subsistence of one in another (like accident in substrate) does not apply here because the distinctions are mental, not external. Thus, the universal and genus are established in the mind, not externally. This refutes the need for an intermediate state.

?

The First Argument for al-Ḥāl
First argument for al-ḥāl with its conclusion of al-wāsiṭa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument for the existence of al-ḥāl (the state) and how it forces an intermediate category between existence and non-existence.

Core Idea
Existence itself cannot be merely existent or non-existent; it must be a state (al-ḥāl).

The debate about al-ḥāl asks whether certain attributes exist in a special intermediate mode. The first argument for al-ḥāl uses a logical dilemma: existence (wujūd) cannot be classified as either existent or non-existent without absurdity, implying an intermediate category (al-wāsiṭa). This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully to grasp the dilemma.
The argument is short but dense.
2
Understand the two horns
Identify why existence cannot be existent (regress) nor non-existent (opposite).
This is a standard argument in kalām.
3
What is al-wāsiṭa?
Realize that rejecting division admits an intermediate, which is the state (al-ḥāl).
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this argument is foundational for affirming al-ḥāl.
It shows the necessity of a third category.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأدلة على ثبوت الحال
قال: احتج المثبتون للحال بوجوده :
الأول: أن الوجود ليس بموجود وإلا تسلسل ولا معدوم وإلا اتصف بنقيضه، والقول بأنه لا يرد عليه القسمة اعتراف بالواسطة .
قلنا : موجود ووجوده عينه، أو معدوم وإنما يلزم الاتصاف بالنقيض، لو كان الوجود عدماً أو الموجد معدماً .
لكان له وجود وتسلسل، ولا معدوم وإلا لا تصف بنقيضه، أي بما يصدق على نقيضه وذلك لأن العدم على تقدير الواسطة ليس نقيضاً للوجود ، بل أخص منه، وإنما نقيضه اللاوجود .
وأجاب صاحب التجريد : بأن الوجود لا يرد عليه القسمة إلى الموجود والمعدوم، فلا يكون أحدهما، ولا يخفى ما فيه من تسليم المدعي والاعتراف بالواسطة .
The evidences for the establishment of the state (al-ḥāl). He said: Those who affirm the state adduce its existence: First: That existence (al-wujūd) is not existent (mawjūd), otherwise there would be infinite regress, nor non-existent (ma'dūm), otherwise it would be described by its opposite. And the claim that it does not fall under the division is an admission of an intermediate (al-wāsiṭa). We said: It is existent and its existence is identical to it, or it is non-existent and being described by the opposite only follows if existence were non-existence or the existent were non-existent. [Objection]: For then it would have existence and infinite regress, and non-existent otherwise it would not be described by its opposite, i.e., by what is true of its opposite, because non-existence upon the assumption of an intermediate is not opposite to existence, but more specific, and its opposite is la-wujūd (non-existence). And the author of al-Tajrīd answered: That existence does not fall under the division into existent and non-existent, so it is neither of them, and it is not hidden what is in it of conceding the claim and admitting the intermediate.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument for the existence of the state (al-ḥāl) and the subsequent replies. The argument states that existence (al-wujūd) itself cannot be classified as either existent (mawjūd) or non-existent (ma'dūm), because if it were existent it would lead to infinite regress, and if non-existent it would be described by its opposite (i.e., non-existence would be a property of existence, which is contradictory). The claim that it does not fall under this division is an admission of an intermediate category (al-wāsiṭa). The author then gives a reply: either existence is existent and its existence is identical to it (so no regress), or it is non-existent and the problem of being described by its opposite only arises if existence were non-existence or the existent were non-existent, which is not the case. An objection is then raised: if we say existence is existent, then it would have an existence and so on ad infinitum; if we say it is non-existent, then it would be described by its opposite. The objection clarifies that non-existence on the assumption of an intermediate is not the opposite of existence but more specific, and its true opposite is la-wujūd (non-existence). Then the author of al-Tajrīd (Naṣīr al-Dīn al-Ṭūsī) gives an answer: existence does not fall under the division into existent and non-existent, so it is neither. However, the author criticizes this as implying the concession of the claim and admission of an intermediate.

Key Terms
الوجود
الموجود
المعدوم
الواسطة
الحال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think al-ḥāl refers to physical states like emotions or temporary conditions.
Correction: In kalām, al-ḥāl is a technical term for an attribute that is neither fully existent nor fully non-existent, often used for relational properties.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, what happens if existence (al-wujūd) is considered existent (mawjūd)?
A) It leads to infinite regress.
B) It is described by its opposite.
C) It becomes non-existent.
D) It becomes an accident.

Key Takeaway
The first argument for al-ḥāl shows that existence must be an intermediate state between existent and non-existent, otherwise contradictions arise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your own existence (wujūd) is not a thing you possess; according to this argument, it is a state that cannot be pinned down as either 'existent' or 'non-existent'. Do you live as though your existence is a gift that you hold, or as a state that depends entirely on the One who truly exists? How would your humility change if you truly internalized that?
?
The argument shows that denying a middle ground is itself an admission of a middle ground. What area of your life—your character, your relationship with Allah, your understanding of good and evil—are you falsely treating as binary when there is a subtle state in between? Will you seek that nuance today?
?
If existence itself is not 'existent', then what does that say about your sense of self and independence? Are you willing to admit that your very 'being' is contingent and momentary, not a fixed thing? How would that reshape your trust in Allah versus your trust in yourself?

Responses to the First Argument for al-Ḥāl
Replies, objection, and Tusi's answer to the first argument for al-ḥāl.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the main responses to the first argument for al-ḥāl, including the reply, objection, and Tusi's answer.

Core Idea
Different responses to the argument reveal deeper issues about existence and the state.

After the first argument, its supporters and critics offered various replies. One reply identifies existence with the existent; an objection raises regress; and Naṣīr al-Dīn al-Ṭūsī denies the division. This lesson examines these responses and their weaknesses.

Guided Path
1
Read the responses passage
Read from 'We said' to the end, including objection and Tusi's answer.
2
Analyze the first reply
Understand the claim that existence is identical to the existent, avoiding regress.
3
Examine the objection
The objection argues that even if existence is existent, it leads to regress; also clarifies opposite.
4
Evaluate Tusi's answer
Tusi says existence does not fall under the division, but this is criticized as conceding the intermediate.

الأدلة على ثبوت الحال
قال: احتج المثبتون للحال بوجوده :
الأول: أن الوجود ليس بموجود وإلا تسلسل ولا معدوم وإلا اتصف بنقيضه، والقول بأنه لا يرد عليه القسمة اعتراف بالواسطة .
قلنا : موجود ووجوده عينه، أو معدوم وإنما يلزم الاتصاف بالنقيض، لو كان الوجود عدماً أو الموجد معدماً .
لكان له وجود وتسلسل، ولا معدوم وإلا لا تصف بنقيضه، أي بما يصدق على نقيضه وذلك لأن العدم على تقدير الواسطة ليس نقيضاً للوجود ، بل أخص منه، وإنما نقيضه اللاوجود .

The evidences for the establishment of the state (al-ḥāl). He said: Those who affirm the state adduce its existence: First: That existence (al-wujūd) is not existent (mawjūd), otherwise there would be infinite regress, nor non-existent (ma'dūm), otherwise it would be described by its opposite. This passage presents the first argument for the existence of the state (al-ḥāl) and the subsequent replies. The argument states that existence (al-wujūd) itself cannot be classified as either existent (mawjūd) or non-existent (ma'dūm), because if it were existent it would lead to infinite regress, and if non-existent it would be described by its opposite (i.e., non-existence would be a property of existence, which is contradictory). 6 min
Objection on the Fixed: Two Answers
Objection on the intermediate with two answers by the Imam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the intermediate must be part of the fixed, and the two answers from the Imam regarding the nature of existence and the description of opposites.

Core Idea
The intermediate between fixed and negated is a logical distinction, not a third category.

This passage tackles a subtle objection in kalam about the nature of the 'fixed' (al-thābit). The objector claims that an intermediate state must belong to one of the two extremes. The Imam responds with two sophisticated answers, one affirming existence as identical to itself and the other allowing description by derivation. Let's follow the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objection that the intermediate must be a division of the fixed. Understand why existence is said to be fixity, not fixed.
Notice the parallel with fixity and negation.
2
Author's initial reply
The author says the excuse is worse than the sin. What does this mean? He asserts that what was mentioned already assumes an intermediate.
This is a meta-objection.
3
Imam's first answer
The Imam chooses that existence is existent and identical to itself, distinguished by a negative qualification – no quiddity.
This avoids infinite regress.
4
Alternative second answer
Another answer: choose existence is non-existent, but description by opposite is only impossible in muwāṭa'a, not ishtiqāq.
This distinguishes types of predication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بين الثابت
فإن قيل : الواسطة يجب أن تكون قسماً من الثابت، والوجود ليس بثابت كما أنه ليس بمنفي، وإنما هو ثبوت، وهذا كما أن كلاً من الثبوت والنفي ، ليس ثابتاً ولا منفياً ولم يلزم من ذلك كونه واسطة بينهما .
قلنا العذر أشد من الجزم ، لأن ما ذكرنا قول بالواسطة : والمنفي بارتفاع النقيضين .
وأجاب الإمام : بأنا نختار أن الوجود موجود، ووجوده عينه لا زائد ليلزم تسلسل الوجودات فامتيازه عن سائر الموجودات يكون بقيد سلبي، هو أن لا ماهية لـه وراء الوجود
وقد يجاب بأنا نختار أنه معدوم واتصاف الشيء بنقيضه إنما يمتنع بطريق المواطأة مثل إن الموجود عدم، والموجود معدوم، وإما بطريق الاشتقاق مثل إن الوجود ذو عدم، فلا نسلم استحالته، فإنه بمنزلة
On the fixed (al-thābit). For if it is said: The intermediate must be a division of the fixed, and existence is not fixed just as it is not negated; rather it is fixity (thubūt). This is like how both fixity and negation are not fixed nor negated, and that does not necessitate it being an intermediate between them. We said: The excuse is worse than the sin, because what we mentioned is a statement with an intermediate, and the negated is by the removal of the two opposites. And the Imām answered: We choose that existence is existent and its existence is identical to it without addition, so that no infinite regress of existences follows. Its distinction from other existents is by a negative qualification, which is that it has no quiddity (māhiyya) apart from existence. And it may be answered by choosing that it is non-existent, and the description of a thing by its opposite is only impossible in the manner of agreement (muwāṭa'a), like 'the existent is non-existence' and 'the existent is non-existent', but in the manner of derivation (ishtiqāq), like 'existence has non-existence', we do not admit its impossibility, for it is like...
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage deals with an objection concerning the 'fixed' (al-thābit) in relation to the intermediate state. The objection states that an intermediate must be a division of what is fixed (thābit), but existence is not fixed; rather it is fixity (thubūt) itself. Just as fixity and negation are not fixed nor negated, but they are not intermediate. The author replies that this excuse is worse than the sin, because what was mentioned (the original argument) is a statement with an intermediate, and the negated (al-manfī) is by the removal of the two opposites (i.e., the intermediate is not a genuine opposite). Then the Imām (presumably Fakhr al-Dīn al-Rāzī) gives an answer: we choose that existence is existent and its existence is identical to it, so there is no infinite regress; its distinction from other existents is by a negative qualification — it has no quiddity beyond existence. An alternative answer is also given: we choose that it is non-existent, and the impossibility of a thing being described by its opposite only applies in the case of 'agreement' (muwāṭa'a), e.g., 'the existent is non-existence' (which is directly contradictory), but not in the case of 'derivation' (ishtiqāq), e.g., 'existence has non-existence' (which is not a direct contradiction). The answer then seems to continue but is cut off.

الواسطة
الثبوت
المنفي
النقيضين
المواطأة
الاشتقاق
الماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'fixed' (al-thābit) refers to eternal objects.
Correction: Here it means a determined category within a division, not necessarily eternal.

Checkpoint
According to the Imām's answer, how is existence distinguished from other existents?
A) By having a quiddity beyond existence.
B) By a negative qualification: it has no quiddity apart from existence.
C) By being non-existent.
D) By being a division of the fixed.

Key Takeaway
The intermediate between fixed and negated is resolved by either identifying existence with its own actuality or by distinguishing between direct and derivative predication of opposites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that the Imām avoids an infinite regress by affirming that existence is identical to itself. In your own life of faith, do you ever add unnecessary layers of complexity to your beliefs, endlessly justifying them instead of accepting a simple, self-evident truth? Where might you be committing 'the excuse is worse than the sin' by overcomplicating what God has made clear?
?
The second answer suggests we can describe a thing by its opposite in a derived sense. Think of a time when you described God in terms that seemed contradictory—like power and mercy, or justice and forgiveness. Do you truly accept that such language is not contradictory but a proper derivative predication, or do you secretly think God must be one or the other? Confess: Are you willing to trust the scholars' precision, or do you cling to your own limited understanding?

intermediate
5 min
The Problem of an Intermediary Between Existence and Non-Existence
The author presents the objection against an intermediary state and defines the true opposite of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why positing an intermediary between existence and non-existence leads to a contradiction and how the true opposite of existence is defined.

Core Idea
The opposite of existence is not non-existence but absolute non-existence (lā-wujūd).

The author introduces a fundamental problem in metaphysics: can there be a state between existence and non-existence? He argues that such an intermediary would either be existent (leading to regress) or non-existent (but then it would be described by its opposite, which is impossible). This leads to a crucial clarification about the nature of opposites.

Guided Path
1
Read the conditional argument
Read the passage and identify the conditional: if an intermediary existed, it would have to be either existent or non-existent.
Notice the distinction between 'being existent' and 'being described by its opposite'.
2
Identify the true opposite
The author clarifies that non-existence (ʿadam) is not the opposite of existence; the true opposite is lā-wujūd (absolute non-existence).
This distinction is key to understanding the rest of the discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكان له وجود وتسلسل، ولا معدوم وإلا لا تصف بنقيضه، أي بما يصدق على نقيضه وذلك لأن العدم على تقدير الواسطة ليس نقيضاً للوجود ، بل أخص منه، وإنما نقيضه اللاوجود. وأجاب صاحب التجريد : بأن الوجود لا يرد عليه القسمة إلى الموجود والمعدوم، فلا يكون أحدهما، ولا يخفى ما فيه من تسليم المدعي والاعتراف بالواسطة . بين الثابت فإن قيل : الواسطة يجب أن تكون قسماً من الثابت، والوجود ليس بثابت كما أنه ليس بمنفي، وإنما هو ثبوت، وهذا كما أن كلاً من الثبوت والنفي ، ليس ثابتاً ولا منفياً ولم يلزم من ذلك كونه واسطة بينهما . قلنا العذر أشد من الجزم، لأن ما ذكرنا قول بالواسطة : والمنفي بارتفاع النقيضين . وأجاب الإمام : بأنا نختار أن الوجود موجود، ووجوده عينه لا زائد ليلزم تسلسل الوجودات فامتيازه عن سائر الموجودات يكون بقيد سلبي، هو أن لا ماهية له وراء الوجود وقد يجاب بأنا نختار أنه معدوم واتصاف الشيء بنقيضه إنما يمتنع بطريق المواطأة مثل إن الموجود عدم، والموجود معدوم، وإما بطريق الاشتقاق مثل إن الوجود ذو عدم، فلا نسلم استحالته، فإنه بمنزلة قولنا الحيوان ذو لا حيوان هو السواد أو البياض وسائر ما يقوم به من الأعراض. والأقرب أنه إن أريد الوجود المطلق فمعدوم أو الخاص كوجود الواجب، ووجود الإنسان فموجود ووجوده زائد عليه، عارض له هو المطلق أو الحصة منه، وليس له وجود آخر ليتسلسل، فإن أريد بكونه موجوداً بوجود هو نفسه هذا المعنى فحق. وإن أريد بمعنى أنه نفسه وجود فلا يدفع بين المعدوم والموجود بمعنى ماله الوجود هذا والحق أن الواسطة هذه لشبهة قوية.
It would have existence and regress, and it is not nonexistent, otherwise it would not be described by its opposite, i.e., by what is true of its opposite, and that is because non-existence, on the assumption of an intermediary, is not the opposite of existence, but rather more specific than it, and its opposite is la-wujud (non-existence). The author of al-Tajrid answered: that existence is not subject to division into existent and nonexistent, so it is not one of them, and it is not hidden what is in this of granting the claim and acknowledging the intermediary. 'Between the established.' For it is said: the intermediary must be a part of the established, and existence is not established just as it is not negated, rather it is establishment (thubut), and this is like that each of establishment and negation is not established nor negated, and it does not necessitate from that its being an intermediary between them. We say: the excuse is stronger than the resolution, because what we mentioned is a statement with the intermediary: and what is negated is the removal of the two opposites. And the Imam answered: that we choose that existence is existent, and its existence is identical to it, not additional, so that a regress of existences would follow; so its distinction from other existents is by a negative condition, which is that it has no quiddity beyond existence. And it may be answered: that we choose that it is nonexistent, and the description of a thing by its opposite is only impossible in the way of agreement (muwata'a), such as 'the existent is non-existence' and 'the existent is nonexistent'; or in the way of derivation (ishtiqaq), such as 'existence possesses non-existence'; so we do not concede its impossibility, for it is like our saying 'the animal which possesses no animal is blackness or whiteness and all the accidents that subsist in it.' And the closest is that if absolute existence is intended, then it is nonexistent; or specific existence, like the existence of the Necessary and the existence of man, then it is existent and its existence is additional to it, an accident to it which is the absolute or a portion of it, and it does not have another existence so that a regress would follow; for if it is intended by its being existent by an existence which is itself this meaning, then it is correct. And if it is intended in the sense that it is itself existence, then it does not refute the distinction between the nonexistent and the existent in the sense of that which has existence. This, and the truth is that this intermediary is due to a strong suspicion.
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage is a complex discussion of the problem of an intermediary state (wāsiṭa) between existence and non-existence. The author begins by stating a conditional: if there were an intermediary between existence and non-existence, it would itself have to be existent (thus leading to regress) or non-existent (but then it would be described by its opposite, which is impossible). The argument explains that non-existence (ʿadam) is not the strict opposite of existence; the opposite is 'la-wujūd' (absolute non-existence). Then the author presents the answer of the author of al-Tajrīd (Naṣīr al-Dīn al-Ṭūsī): existence itself cannot be divided into existent and non-existent, so it cannot be placed in either category. This answer is criticized for conceding the existence of an intermediary. An objection is then raised: the intermediary must be a part of the established (thābit), but existence is not established—it is establishment (thubūt) itself, just as negation is not negated. The author responds that this objection is weaker than the original claim. Then the Imām (Fakhr al-Dīn al-Rāzī?) offers two answers: first, that existence is existent and identical to its existence (to avoid regress), and its distinction is by a negative condition (lā māhiyya lahu). Second, that existence is non-existent, and describing a thing by its opposite is only impossible in one way (muwāṭa'a) but not in another (ishtiqāq). The author then gives the closest view: absolute existence is non-existent, while specific existence (e.g., the Necessary's existence, man's existence) is existent with an additional existence that is accidental. The truth, the author concludes, is that the intermediary is due to a strong suspicion (shubha).

Key Terms
وجود
عدم
واسطة
نقيض
لاوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the true opposite of existence?
A) Non-existence (ʿadam)
B) Absolute non-existence (lā-wujūd)
C) Intermediary
D) Both A and B

Key Takeaway
The problem of an intermediary is based on a misunderstanding of what the opposite of existence truly is.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your life have you accepted a comfortable 'middle ground'—something that is neither true nor false, neither belief nor disbelief—and how does this lesson challenge you to call it what it is?
?
The author shows that confusing ‘non-existence’ with ‘absolute non-existence’ leads to a false problem. Where might you be using imprecise language about your faith to avoid facing a hard truth about yourself?

intermediate
6 min
Al-Ṭūsī's Answer and the Objection of the Established
The author presents al-Ṭūsī's answer and the subsequent dialectic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ṭūsī's argument that existence is not subject to division, and the subsequent criticism that this concedes an intermediary.

Core Idea
Existence cannot be divided into existent and non-existent, so it cannot be an intermediary.

In response to the problem, the author of al-Tajrid (Naṣīr al-Dīn al-Ṭūsī) argues that existence itself is not subject to the binary division, so it cannot be placed in either category. However, critics object that this answer actually concedes an intermediary state. The passage also discusses the concept of 'established' (thābit) and whether existence is established or not.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Ṭūsī's answer
Existence is not subject to division into existent and non-existent, so it is neither.
This is a direct response to the initial problem.
2
Understand the objection
An intermediary must be part of the established, but existence is not established—it is establishment itself.
The objector tries to show that al-Ṭūsī's answer still implies an intermediary.
3
See the author's rebuttal
The objection is weaker because it itself posits an intermediary between establishment and negation.
The author defends al-Ṭūsī's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكان له وجود وتسلسل، ولا معدوم وإلا لا تصف بنقيضه، أي بما يصدق على نقيضه وذلك لأن العدم على تقدير الواسطة ليس نقيضاً للوجود ، بل أخص منه، وإنما نقيضه اللاوجود. وأجاب صاحب التجريد : بأن الوجود لا يرد عليه القسمة إلى الموجود والمعدوم، فلا يكون أحدهما، ولا يخفى ما فيه من تسليم المدعي والاعتراف بالواسطة . بين الثابت فإن قيل : الواسطة يجب أن تكون قسماً من الثابت، والوجود ليس بثابت كما أنه ليس بمنفي، وإنما هو ثبوت، وهذا كما أن كلاً من الثبوت والنفي ، ليس ثابتاً ولا منفياً ولم يلزم من ذلك كونه واسطة بينهما . قلنا العذر أشد من الجزم، لأن ما ذكرنا قول بالواسطة : والمنفي بارتفاع النقيضين . وأجاب الإمام : بأنا نختار أن الوجود موجود، ووجوده عينه لا زائد ليلزم تسلسل الوجودات فامتيازه عن سائر الموجودات يكون بقيد سلبي، هو أن لا ماهية له وراء الوجود وقد يجاب بأنا نختار أنه معدوم واتصاف الشيء بنقيضه إنما يمتنع بطريق المواطأة مثل إن الموجود عدم، والموجود معدوم، وإما بطريق الاشتقاق مثل إن الوجود ذو عدم، فلا نسلم استحالته، فإنه بمنزلة قولنا الحيوان ذو لا حيوان هو السواد أو البياض وسائر ما يقوم به من الأعراض. والأقرب أنه إن أريد الوجود المطلق فمعدوم أو الخاص كوجود الواجب، ووجود الإنسان فموجود ووجوده زائد عليه، عارض له هو المطلق أو الحصة منه، وليس له وجود آخر ليتسلسل، فإن أريد بكونه موجوداً بوجود هو نفسه هذا المعنى فحق. وإن أريد بمعنى أنه نفسه وجود فلا يدفع بين المعدوم والموجود بمعنى ماله الوجود هذا والحق أن الواسطة هذه لشبهة قوية.
It would have existence and regress, and it is not nonexistent, otherwise it would not be described by its opposite, i.e., by what is true of its opposite, and that is because non-existence, on the assumption of an intermediary, is not the opposite of existence, but rather more specific than it, and its opposite is la-wujud (non-existence). The author of al-Tajrid answered: that existence is not subject to division into existent and nonexistent, so it is not one of them, and it is not hidden what is in this of granting the claim and acknowledging the intermediary. 'Between the established.' For it is said: the intermediary must be a part of the established, and existence is not established just as it is not negated, rather it is establishment (thubut), and this is like that each of establishment and negation is not established nor negated, and it does not necessitate from that its being an intermediary between them. We say: the excuse is stronger than the resolution, because what we mentioned is a statement with the intermediary: and what is negated is the removal of the two opposites. And the Imam answered: that we choose that existence is existent, and its existence is identical to it, not additional, so that a regress of existences would follow; so its distinction from other existents is by a negative condition, which is that it has no quiddity beyond existence. And it may be answered: that we choose that it is nonexistent, and the description of a thing by its opposite is only impossible in the way of agreement (muwata'a), such as 'the existent is non-existence' and 'the existent is nonexistent'; or in the way of derivation (ishtiqaq), such as 'existence possesses non-existence'; so we do not concede its impossibility, for it is like our saying 'the animal which possesses no animal is blackness or whiteness and all the accidents that subsist in it.' And the closest is that if absolute existence is intended, then it is nonexistent; or specific existence, like the existence of the Necessary and the existence of man, then it is existent and its existence is additional to it, an accident to it which is the absolute or a portion of it, and it does not have another existence so that a regress would follow; for if it is intended by its being existent by an existence which is itself this meaning, then it is correct. And if it is intended in the sense that it is itself existence, then it does not refute the distinction between the nonexistent and the existent in the sense of that which has existence. This, and the truth is that this intermediary is due to a strong suspicion.
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage is a complex discussion of the problem of an intermediary state (wāsiṭa) between existence and non-existence. The author begins by stating a conditional: if there were an intermediary between existence and non-existence, it would itself have to be existent (thus leading to regress) or non-existent (but then it would be described by its opposite, which is impossible). The argument explains that non-existence (ʿadam) is not the strict opposite of existence; the opposite is 'la-wujūd' (absolute non-existence). Then the author presents the answer of the author of al-Tajrīd (Naṣīr al-Dīn al-Ṭūsī): existence itself cannot be divided into existent and non-existent, so it cannot be placed in either category. This answer is criticized for conceding the existence of an intermediary. An objection is then raised: the intermediary must be a part of the established (thābit), but existence is not established—it is establishment (thubūt) itself, just as negation is not negated. The author responds that this objection is weaker than the original claim. Then the Imām (Fakhr al-Dīn al-Rāzī?) offers two answers: first, that existence is existent and identical to its existence (to avoid regress), and its distinction is by a negative condition (lā māhiyya lahu). Second, that existence is non-existent, and describing a thing by its opposite is only impossible in one way (muwāṭa'a) but not in another (ishtiqāq). The author then gives the closest view: absolute existence is non-existent, while specific existence (e.g., the Necessary's existence, man's existence) is existent with an additional existence that is accidental. The truth, the author concludes, is that the intermediary is due to a strong suspicion (shubha).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that existence can be classified as either existent or non-existent.
Correction: Al-Ṭūsī argues that existence itself is beyond this division.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of al-Ṭūsī's answer?
A) Existence is existent
B) Existence is nonexistent
C) Existence is not subject to division
D) Existence is an intermediary

Key Takeaway
Al-Ṭūsī's response, though criticized, shows that existence is a unique concept not subject to the binary of existent/non-existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If existence itself is not something you can point to or categorize, then your own existence is not an attribute you possess—it is the very ground of your being. Are you living with gratitude for the sheer fact that you exist, or do you only appreciate the things that fill your existence?
?
The passage shows that a simple category mistake (treating existence as a third state) can lead to confusion. What assumptions about your own life might be similarly mistaken? What would it mean to examine those assumptions with the same dialectical rigor al-Ṭūsī applies here?
?
You cannot separate yourself from your existence; it precedes every description. Does your daily awareness reflect this reality, or do you live as if your existence were just one more thing to be managed? How would your priorities change if you truly meditated on the fact that you are, before you are anything else?

Rational Judgments
advanced
8 min
The Imam's Two Answers and the Distinction Between Absolute and Specific Existence
The author presents the Imam's two answers and the final resolution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two answers of the Imam (al-Rāzī) regarding whether existence is existent or non-existent, and the final resolution distinguishing absolute existence from specific existence.

Core Idea
Absolute existence is non-existent; only specific existences have existence added to their quiddities.

The Imam (Fakhr al-Dīn al-Rāzī) provides two possible answers to the problem. First, existence is existent and identical to its existence, avoiding regress. Second, existence is non-existent, and describing a thing by its opposite is not always impossible. The author then gives the closest view: absolute existence is non-existent, while specific existences (like the Necessary's existence) are existent with an additional existence. This resolves the confusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the Imam's first answer
Existence is existent and identical to its existence, distinguished by a negative condition (no quiddity).
2
Read the second answer
Existence is non-existent; describing a thing by its opposite is only impossible in muwāṭa'a, not ishtiqāq.
3
Understand the closest view
Distinguish between absolute existence (non-existent) and specific existence (additional accident).
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The intermediary is due to a strong suspicion; it is not a real state.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Existence is a single concept that can be either existent or non-existent.
Correction: Absolute existence is non-existent; only specific existence is existent.

Checkpoint
According to the closest view, what is the status of absolute existence?
A) Existent
B) Non-existent
C) Intermediary
D) Both existent and non-existent

Distinguishing absolute existence from specific existence resolves the problem of an intermediary.
